Sources of Critique of Logical Principles
Author traces critiques of logical principles across traditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main sources of critique against the principles of non-contradiction and excluded middle as presented in the passage, and distinguish between external borrowing and independent development.

Core Idea
Critiques of logic arose independently in different traditions, not from borrowing.

This passage explores whether Muslim critiques of foundational logical principles were borrowed from Greek philosophers or arose from their own investigations. The author argues for independence, citing examples from Greek pre-Socratics, the Muslim scholar al-Sijistani, and contemporary mathematicians. Understanding this helps us see how logical principles are evaluated across cultures and disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Principles
Locate the two logical principles under discussion: the principle of non-contradiction (عدم الجمع بين النقيضين) and the principle of excluded middle (مبدأ الثالث المرفوع). Note their definitions in the key terms.
These are foundational in both Aristotelian logic and Islamic logic.
2
List the Greek Philosophers
Find the four groups or thinkers Aristotle mentions: the Megarians, Heraclitus, Protagoras, and Anaxagoras. Summarize their critiques briefly.
This shows that critiques of logic are not a modern phenomenon.
3
Understand the Author's Question
Identify the central question: Did Muslims borrow these critiques from Greek sources? The author introduces this to challenge a common assumption.
4
Analyze the Evidence for Independence
Examine the author's two arguments: (1) Al-Sijistani's explicit critique arising from his own investigations, (2) Parallel developments in contemporary mathematics without external influence.
The author emphasizes 'nature of the investigations themselves' as the driving force.
5
Evaluate the Conclusion
Assess whether the author successfully demonstrates independence. Note that the passage is historical, not logical, so the conclusion is plausible but not apodictic.
Consider how this impacts the study of Islamic intellectual history.

Sources of this critique: The theologians who did not accept the critique of the principle of non-contradiction and the principle of the impossibility of the simultaneous absence of opposites indicated that this critique is sophistry and impossibility. Does this indication point to the source from which this critique was derived? Or more precisely, did the Muslims take this critique from some philosophical schools before Aristotle, like the Sophists, and some schools after him, like the Sceptics? For both of these groups criticized the principle of non-contradiction and the principle of the impossibility of the simultaneous absence of opposites. Aristotle mentions: 1. That among the philosophers (perhaps he means the Megarians) are those who say that a single thing may exist from one aspect and from another aspect that this can be perceived. Then he mentions that many of the naturalists adopt this idea. 2. That Heraclitus asserted the conjunction of opposites. 3. That Protagoras claims that man is the measure of all things. This leads to saying that what appears to each individual is the truth itself, and that a single thing both exists and does not exist, and that it is at the same time good and evil, and that contradictory assertions are equally true. 4. That Anaxagoras said there is an intermediate, i.e., that opposites may be absent simultaneously. And Aristotle denied that a thing can exist and not exist at the same time. He mentioned that the logical impossibility of negating and affirming the predicate simultaneously for the subject is based on the existential impossibility of the meeting of contradictions in existence... And Aristotle defended the principle of non-contradiction with seven arguments, and he also defended the principle of the non-conjunction of opposites. Indeed, Aristotle devoted the fourth book of his 'Metaphysics' to the defense of these two principles or laws, and a large part of the eleventh book also includes a defense of these two principles. Thus, there were philosophers who criticized these two principles. Aristotle mentioned this in his book 'Metaphysics'. And it is certain that the Muslims knew this book and wrote many commentaries on it. Can we then trace in it the source of the critique of these two principles? I cannot go that far. The reason is clear: the Muslims did not first deviate from these principles. Rather, their investigations led to deviation from them. And the upholders of the state of affairs noted this, so they tried to get rid of the situation. As for al-Sijistani - and although he differs from the former in that he explicitly acknowledges the critique of the principle of the impossibility of the simultaneous absence of opposites and deviation from it - he tries to deny vehemently that he combined opposites, thus he criticized these two principles in general. And the principle of the excluded middle in particular is a specific critique, to which the nature of the investigations themselves led. And its proponents were not influenced by any external influence. And just as happened in the Islamic school, it happened in the contemporary mathematical school in Europe. E. Critique of this law by contemporaries: In the investigations of contemporary mathematicians on mathematical proof, there is a critique of these two principles, especially the principle of the excluded middle. The issue of these laws was placed in a different position than before. Then the matter ended with the rejection of non-contradiction and the principle of the excluded middle, and the rejection of the universal general property attributed to the latter. And they said that there is an intermediate between opposites. And we cannot trace the source of these investigations to the Sophists, for the critique only occurred under the influence of purely mathematical investigations. Moreover, some contemporary logicians did not critique these two

Commentary
This passage is a historical-critical survey of the sources of critique against two foundational logical principles: the principle of non-contradiction (عدم الجمع بين النقيضين) and the principle of excluded middle (مبدأ الثالث المرفوع). The author begins by noting that Muslim theologians who rejected the critique labeled it as sophistry and impossibility. The central question is whether Muslim critics derived their ideas from earlier Greek philosophers like the Sophists or later Sceptics. The author then references Aristotle's 'Metaphysics', where Aristotle records four groups: the Megarians (who claimed a thing can exist from one aspect and not from another), Heraclitus (who asserted the conjunction of opposites), Protagoras (whose relativism leads to contradictions being equally true), and Anaxagoras (who posited an intermediate, denying the excluded middle). Aristotle defended the principles with seven arguments and devoted entire books to their defense. The author then questions whether Muslims borrowed from Aristotle, but argues that Muslim critiques arose independently from their own investigations, not from external influence. He cites al-Sijistani as a Muslim who explicitly criticized the principle of excluded middle but denied combining opposites. Finally, the author notes a parallel development in contemporary mathematics, where mathematicians rejected both principles based on purely mathematical reasoning, not sophistical influence. The passage emphasizes that critiques of these principles emerged autonomously in different intellectual traditions.

Independence of Muslim Critiques of Logical Principles
Historical argument for independence of Muslim logical critiques

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's argument that Muslim critiques of non-contradiction and excluded middle were independent and predated modern European critiques.

Core Idea
Muslim logicians critiqued Aristotle's principles from within, not from external influence.

This lesson examines a historical claim: that Muslim thinkers independently criticized the fundamental logical principles of non-contradiction and excluded middle before modern mathematicians. The author traces both Islamic and modern European critiques, arguing that the Muslim critiques arose from the nature of their own research, not from Greek sources. You will analyze the evidence and reasoning behind this independence thesis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
The author asserts that Muslim thinkers criticized the principles of non-contradiction and excluded middle independently, and that their critiques predate similar European critiques. Highlight the sentences where he states this most directly.
2
Extract evidence for independence
Find the reasons the author gives for believing the Muslim critiques were independent: he mentions that Muslims knew Aristotle's work but did not derive their critique from it; rather, their own researches led to the departure. Also note the example of al-Sijistani.
3
Compare Muslim and modern critiques
The author describes modern mathematical critiques as arising from purely mathematical research, just as Islamic critiques came from internal research. Note the parallel he draws and the claim that Muslims preceded Europeans.
4
Evaluate the argument's strength
Assess whether the author provides sufficient evidence for his claim. Does he cite specific texts or arguments from Muslim thinkers? Is the claim plausible? Consider what more would be needed to prove independence conclusively.

الرابعة من كتاب « ما بعد الطبيعة » كما أن جزءاً كبيراً من المقالة الحادية عشرة يشمل أيضاً دفاعاً عن هذين المبدأين . إذن هناك من الفلاسفة من نقد هذين المبدأين . وذكر أرسطو هذا في كتابه ما بعد الطبيعة . ومن المؤكد أن المسلمين عرفوا هذا الكتاب ، وكتبوا عليه شروحاً كثيرة . فهل يمكننا أن نتلمس فيه مصدر النقد لهذين المبدأين ؟ لا أستطيع أن أذهب إلى هذا الحد . والسبب واضح ، هو أن المسلمين لم يضعوا الخروج عن هذين المبدأين أولاً . بل أدت أبحاثهم إلى الخروج عليها . وراع مثبتو الحال هذا ، فحاولوا التخلص من الموقف . أما السجستاني - The fourth [book] of the Metaphysics, as a large part of the eleventh treatise also includes a defense of these two principles. So there are among the philosophers those who criticized these two principles. Aristotle mentioned this in his book Metaphysics. And it is certain that the Muslims knew this book and wrote many commentaries on it. Can we then trace in it the source of criticism of these two principles? I cannot go that far. The reason is clear: the Muslims did not first set forth the departure from these two principles. Rather, their researches led to departure from them. And the proponents of the state [of affairs] considered this, so they tried to extricate themselves from the situation. As for al-Sijistani—although he differs from the earlier ones in that he explicitly acknowledges criticism of the principle of non-contradiction and departure from it—he nevertheless tries vehemently to deny that he combined contradictories, thus criticizing these two principles in general. And the principle of the excluded middle in particular, a special criticism, the nature of the researches themselves led to it. And its proponents were not influenced by any external influence. And just as it occurred in the Islamic school, it occurred in the contemporary mathematical school in Europe. E. The criticism of contemporaries of this law: In the researches of contemporary mathematicians on mathematical proof there is criticism of these two principles, especially the principle of the excluded middle. The issue of these laws has been placed in a different position from what it was. Then it ended with the rejection of non-contradiction and the principle of the excluded middle and the rejection of the general universal characteristic attributed to the latter. And they said that there is an intermediate between the two contradictories (1). And we cannot trace the source of these researches to the Sophists, for the criticism occurred only under the influence of purely mathematical researches. Moreover, some contemporary logicians did not only criticize these two principles, but also criticized the principle of identity. They advanced objections to the earlier ones that almost approach the objections of the Islamic schools (1). And others denied the restriction of the laws of thought to three, because it is not reasonable that they are all the laws of thought. And others denied that they are self-evident abstract principles, presupposing their prior existence—and upon which the reality of knowledge is based—rather they consider them postulates or general properties of known reality and return in essence to experience. Finally, we were able to show that the thinkers of Islam criticized the principle of the excluded middle with a criticism that is not inconsistent, just as they did in criticizing the principle of combining contradictories, and that the Muslims preceded the Europeans in criticizing this very principle, i.e., the principle of non-contradiction, with a criticism based on the nature of their own researches. And we will see in the next chapter a position of the Muslims towards a self-evident Aristotelian principle, which is the principle of causality.

Commentary
This passage presents a historical argument that Muslim philosophers independently critiqued the logical principles of non-contradiction (مبدأ عدم ارتفاع النقيضين) and the excluded middle (مبدأ الثالث المرفوع) before modern European thinkers. The author first notes that Aristotle's Metaphysics includes defense of these principles and acknowledges some philosophers criticized them. Muslims knew Aristotle's work but their critique arose from their own inquiries, not external influence. The author cites al-Sijistani as an example of a Muslim who explicitly criticized non-contradiction but denied combining contradictories. The passage then discusses modern mathematical critiques of the same principles, arguing these arose from purely mathematical research, not sophistry. Some contemporary logicians also criticized the principle of identity. The author concludes that Muslim thinkers predated Europeans in criticizing these principles, and their criticism stemmed from the nature of their own research.

The Ash'ari Critique of Aristotelian Causality
Author introduces Aristotelian causality and Islamic reactions, focusing on Ash'ari critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Ash'ari position on causality from Aristotle's necessitarian view and identify the historical context of al-Ghazali's occasionalism.

Core Idea
Ash'aris rejected Aristotle's necessitarian causality, not causality itself.

This lesson sets the stage for understanding the Islamic critique of Aristotelian logic. The passage introduces Aristotle's principle of causality as a self-evident logical law, adopted by Islamic Peripatetics like Ibn Rushd. It then pivots to the Ash'ari opposition, clarifying that they did not deny causality entirely but formulated a theory compatible with occasionalism, culminating in al-Ghazali. This distinction is crucial for later debates on necessary connection and divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Identify Aristotle's claim about causality
Find where the passage states Aristotle considered causality as absolutely primary and self-evident, and as a logical law foundational to all logic.
This is the target of the critique.
2
Trace the transmission to Islamic thought
Notice how the passage describes the acceptance of Aristotle's idea among the Peripatetic school (al-mashsha'iyya), especially by Ibn Rushd who prioritized it.
This shows the dominant philosophical position.
3
Locate the Ash'ari objection
Find where the passage says the Ash'aris took a position of denial and violent attack against Aristotelian causality.
4
Understand the nuance in Ash'ari denial
The passage explicitly warns against thinking Ash'aris denied causality completely; they only denied 'Aristotle's conception' and replaced it with their own theory.
This nuance is key: they rejected necessitarianism, not causality as such.
5
Identify the culmination in al-Ghazali
Note that the Ash'ari theory reached its peak with Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, and the passage will trace his theory in subsequent sections.
Al-Ghazali is the central figure in this critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ السَّابع نقد قانون العلية الأرسططاليسية اعتبر أرسطو قانون العلية من المقدمات الأولية بالإطلاق . فلا يمكن القدح في بداهته . وقد عالج أرسطو العلية لا على أنها فقط مبدأ أو مشكلة طبيعية أو ميتافيزيقية ، بل أيضاً على اعتبار أنها قانون عقلي منطقي ، تستند عليه أبحاث المنطق جميعاً(١) . وقد انتقلت فكرة العلية في أبحاث أرسطو إلى العالم الإسلامي . ووجدت قبولاً لدى مدرسة الإسلام المشائية ، بل إن أكبر فلاسفة الإسلام المشائين ، وهو ابن رشد ، يضعها في أبحاثه في المكان الأول(٢) . ولكن سرعان ما وقف مفكرو الإسلام - الممثلون الحقيقيون للإسلام - وهم الأشاعرة - من العلية الأرسططاليسية موقف الإنكار لها والهجوم العنيف عليها . ومن الخطأ القول إن الأشاعرة أنكروا العلية إنكاراً كاملاً ، إنهم أنكروا فقط « تصور أرسطو » لها ووضعوا نظرية في العلية تتوافق مع عناصر المذهب توافقاً تاماً وبلغت أوج نضجها لدى مفكر الأشاعرة الكبير : أبي حامد الغزالي . أولاً موقف المسلمين من هذا القانون : ولكي نتأدى إلى نظرية أبي حامد الغزالي ، علينا أن نتتبع موقف
Chapter Seven: Critique of the Aristotelian Principle of Causality. Aristotle considered the principle of causality as one of the absolutely primary premises. So it cannot be impugned in its self-evidence. And Aristotle treated causality not only as a principle or a natural or metaphysical problem, but also as a rational logical law upon which all logical investigations rely(1). And the idea of causality in Aristotle's investigations transferred to the Islamic world. And it found acceptance among the Peripatetic school of Islam, indeed the greatest of the Peripatetic philosophers of Islam, Ibn Rushd, places it in the first position in his investigations(2). But soon the thinkers of Islam - the true representatives of Islam - and they are the Ash'aris - took a position of denial and violent attack against Aristotelian causality. And it is wrong to say that the Ash'aris denied causality completely; they only denied 'Aristotle's conception' of it and they formulated a theory of causality that completely conforms to the elements of the school, reaching its peak of maturity in the great Ash'ari thinker: Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. First: The Position of Muslims regarding this Law: And in order to approach the theory of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, we must trace the position
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage opens the seventh chapter on the critique of Aristotelian causality. The author states that Aristotle regarded the principle of causality as absolutely primary and self-evident, treating it not only as a metaphysical principle but also as a logical law foundational to all logic. The idea entered the Islamic world and was accepted by the Peripatetic school, notably by Ibn Rushd who prioritized it. However, the Ash'ari theologians, described as the true representatives of Islam, strongly opposed Aristotle's conception of causality. The author clarifies that the Ash'aris did not deny causality per se but rejected Aristotle's specific formulation, replacing it with a theory consistent with Ash'ari theology, culminating in al-Ghazali's mature theory. The passage ends by indicating that to understand al-Ghazali's theory, one must trace the Muslim position on this law, setting up the subsequent analysis.

قانون العلية (qanun al-'illiyya)
المشائية (al-mashsha'iyya)
الأشاعرة (al-asha'ira)

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari critique of Aristotelian causality does not reject causality per se but replaces necessitarianism with an occasionalist framework consistent with divine omnipotence.

The Ash'ari Theory of Continuous Creation and the Critique of Natural Causality
Author presents the Ash'ari response to the Quranic rejection of causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Ash'ari doctrine of continuous new creation (al-khalq al-jadīd al-mustamir) undermines the Aristotelian concept of necessary causation and reconstruct the logical steps of their argument.

Core Idea
Continuous creation disconnects cause and effect, making God the direct creator of every event.

The Ash'ari school, inspired by the Quran's rejection of causality, developed a coherent metaphysical system to explain events without necessary causal connections. Their doctrine of continuous new creation holds that each moment is independent, created directly by God. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning behind this view and its implications for the Islamic critique of causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic foundation
Notice how the author begins by stating that the Quran denies causality and challenges 'causes'. This provides the theological basis for the Ash'ari position.
2
Analyze the Ash'ari problem
The Ash'aris recognized a tension: they accepted substance (jawhar) and the non-continuance of accidents (aʿrāḍ). How could this be compatible with causality? Their solution was continuous creation.
3
Understand continuous creation
Continuous creation means every event is composed of discrete atomic parts, each created by God. There is no connection between parts via cause and effect; they are independent.
4
Distinguish human and natural actions
For the Ash'aris, natural events have no choice, while human actions involve acquisition (kasb). Human will is effective through God's creation of parts, preserving moral responsibility.
5
Assess the coherence of the system
The author claims this system integrates metaphysics, physics, and ethics. Reflect on how the denial of necessary causality is consistent with observable regularity (the theory of habit).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلمين منذ نشأة الفكر الإسلامي من العلية بمعناها الأرسططاليسي . وأول ما يقابلنا في هذه النشأة ، هو موقف القرآن نفسه . ومن الواضح أن القرآن في مواضع عديدة ينكر العلية ، ويتحدى « الأسباب » ويفكر « الصعود » إليها ، هذا الموقف القرآني تجاه الأسباب ، أطلق مشيخة الأشاعرة في تدعيم إنكارها ، والقضاء على تصورها الميتافيزيقي . تساءل الأشاعرة : كيف يمكن التوفيق بين اعتناقهم لفكرة الجوهر ، وتصورهم لعدم بقاء الأعراض وقتين وبين فكرة العلية ، وأن لكل سبب مسبباً في ذاته . إن فكرة الخلق الجديد المستمر ، وهي النتيجة الحتمية لتصور أن الأعراض لا تبقى زمانين ـ تعني أن كل حادث ينقسم إلى أجزاء منفصلة مستقل بعضها عن بعض تمام الإستقلال ، ولا تتصل هذه الأجزاء المنفصلة بعضها ببعض اتصال علة بمعلول أو سبب بمسبب . إن هذه الأجزاء يخلقها الله دائماً ، سواء كانت سواء أكانت فعلاً إنسانياً أو فعلاً طبيعياً . فعالم الطبيعة وعالم الإنسان محكومان بهذه الأجزاء ، غير أن عالم الإنسان يختلف عند الأشاعرة عن عالم الطبيعة كثيراً ، فبينما لا يشعر هذا العالم الطبيعي بالاختيار ، يشعر الثاني بأنه يكتسب هذه الأجزاء التي يفعلها الله فيه أو يخلقها فيه . فالإرادة الإنسانية إذن مؤثرة بخلق الله فيها للأجزاء وهكذا أقام الأشاعرة والأوائل نظاماً بديعاً للعلية متماسكاً ومتكاملاً . يستند على بحث ميتافيزيقي وطبيعي وأخلاقي .
أما المعتزلة ـ من أمثال أبي الهذيل العلاف وأبي هاشم الجبائي وغيرهما ، فقد أنكروا بوضوح العلية الأرسططاليسية وتصورها في مجال العالم الطبيعي ، ولكنهم وقد نادوا بحرية الإرادة الإنسانية ، وأن الإنسان هو الفاعل على الحقيقة ، فلم يسيروا بتصور العلية الجديد حتى نهايته . فجاءت نتائج مذهبهم في تفسير القدرة الإلهية والعالم الطبيعي مشوشة مضطربة .
سار الأشاعرة بمباحثهم في العلية قدماً ، فأنتجوا لنا نظرية العادة وجريانها على أكمل صورة لفكرة العلية الإسلامية إنما نجدها لدى مفكر الأشاعرة الكبير أبي بكر الباقلاني ( المتوفي عام ٤٠٣ هـ ) فقد اعترف الباقلاني « بالعلية بمعناها العام المطلق » واعترف بأن القدرة الإلهية هي علة وجود العالم ولكن بشكل خاص يختلف اختلافاً عن القائلين بأن « صانع العالم طبيعة من الطبائع وجب حدوث العالم عن وجودها » لأن هذه الطبيعة القديمة التي يذهب إليها القائلون بفعل الطبائع لا تخلو أن تكون « معنى موجوداً » أو معدوماً ليس بشيء » فإن كانت « معنى معدوماً ليس بشيء » لم يجز أن تفعل شيئاً ، أو أن يكون عنها أو ينسب إليها شيء « لأنه لو جاز ذلك ، جاز وجود الحوادث من كل معدوم وعن كل معدوم » وذلك باطل باتفاق . أما إن كانت الطبيعة التي ينسب إليها حدوث العالم ويتعلق حدوث العالم بها معنى موجوداً ، لم تخل تلك الطبيعة من أن تكون قديمة أو محدثة ، فان كانت قديمة ، وجب أن تكون الحوادث الكائنة عنها قديمة مثلها ، « لأن الطبيعة لم تزل موجودة ولا مانع من وجود الحوادث الموجبة عنها ، فيجب وجود الحوادث الموجبة عنها ، فيجب وجودها مع الطبيعة في القدم ، كما يجب اعتماد الحجر مع وجود طبعه ، وإحراق النار وانحلال الطبع والإسكار مع وجود طبع النار والسقمونيا والشراب ، إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع ، فكذلك يجب وجود العالم في القدم ، وإن كان محدثاً ، مع وجود الطبع الكائن عندهم » إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع (۱) ولكن هناك استحالة متفق عليها بين مختلف العقلاء - على إستحالة قدم الحوادث ، وهذا دليل على أنها لا تجوز أن تكون حادثة عن طبيعة قديمة .
ولكن ألا يلزم هذا الباقلاني - في قوله هو والأشاعرة - إن صانع العالم لم يزل قادراً على ايجاده ، لأن قدرته على الإيجاد قديمة . ويرد الباقلاني على هذا بأن هذا لا يجب من وجهين :
أولاً : يقرر الباقلاني أنه لا يقول بأن الله قادر بقدرة في الأزل على أن تكون الأفعال مع القدرة ، وإنما هو يذهب إلى أن الله قادر على « أن يستأنف الأفعال وعلى أن يحدثها في زمان كانت قبله معدومة ، ومحال أن تكون قدرة
Muslims since the beginning of Islamic thought [rejected] causality in its Aristotelian sense. The first thing we encounter in this beginning is the stance of the Quran itself. It is clear that the Quran in many places denies causality, challenges 'causes', and considers 'ascending' to them. This Quranic stance towards causes liberated the Ash'ari masters to strengthen its denial and eliminate its metaphysical conception. The Ash'aris asked: How can we reconcile their adherence to the idea of substance, and their conception of the non-continuance of accidents over two moments, with the idea of causality, that every cause has an effect in itself? The idea of continuous new creation, which is the inevitable result of the conception that accidents do not remain for two moments, means that every event is divided into separate parts, each completely independent of the other, and these separate parts are not connected to each other by the connection of cause to effect or reason to result. These parts are always created by God, whether they are a human action or a natural action. The world of nature and the world of man are governed by these parts, but the world of man differs according to the Ash'aris greatly from the world of nature. While this natural world does not feel choice, the second feels that it acquires these parts that God does in it or creates in it. Human will therefore is effective by God's creation of parts in it. Thus the Ash'aris and the early ones established a wonderful system of causality, coherent and integrated, based on metaphysical, natural, and ethical inquiry. As for the Mu'tazila—like Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf and Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i and others—they clearly denied Aristotelian causality and its conception in the natural world, but since they advocated for human free will and that man is the true agent, they did not follow the new conception of causality to its end. So the results of their school in explaining divine power and the natural world came out confused and disturbed. The Ash'aris advanced their investigations into causality, producing for us the theory of habit (al-'adah). Its most perfect form of the Islamic idea of causality is found with the great Ash'ari thinker Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403 AH). Al-Baqillani acknowledged 'causality in its general absolute sense' and acknowledged that divine power is the cause of the existence of the world, but in a specific way different from those who say 'the maker of the world is a nature among natures, and the occurrence of the world is necessitated by its existence'. For this eternal nature that the proponents of the action of natures hold to must either be 'an existing meaning' or 'non-existent, not a thing'. If it is 'a non-existent meaning, not a thing', it cannot do anything, nor can anything come from it or be attributed to it, 'because if that were permissible, the occurrence of events from every non-existent and from every non-existent would be permissible', and that is false by consensus. If the nature to which the occurrence of the world is attributed and upon which the occurrence of the world depends is an existing meaning, then that nature must be either eternal or originated. If it is eternal, then the events coming from it must be eternal like it, 'because the nature has always existed and there is no obstacle to the occurrence of the events necessitated by it, so the occurrence of the events necessitated by it is necessary, so their existence with the nature in eternity is necessary, just as the downward motion of a stone is necessary given its nature, and the burning of fire and dissolution of a temperament and intoxication given the nature of fire, scammony, and wine, if there is no obstacle preventing that. Similarly, the existence of the world in eternity would be necessary, and if it is originated, [it would require] the existence of the nature as they claim' if there is no obstacle preventing that. But there is an agreed-upon impossibility among various rational minds—the impossibility of the eternity of events. This is proof that they cannot be originated from an eternal nature. But does this not obligate al-Baqillani—in his statement and the Ash'aris—that the maker of the world has always been able to bring it into existence, because his power to bring into existence is eternal? Al-Baqillani responds to this that this is not necessary for two reasons: First: al-Baqillani affirms that he does not say that God is powerful with an eternal power such that actions are concomitant with power, but rather he holds that God is powerful 'to initiate actions and to bring them into being in a time in which they were previously non-existent, and it is impossible for power [to be]...'
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the Islamic theological schools' engagement with the Aristotelian concept of causality. The author begins by noting the Quran's rejection of causality, which set the stage for the Ash'ari school to deny the metaphysical necessity of causes. The Ash'aris faced an internal tension: how to reconcile their acceptance of substance (jawhar) and the non-continuance of accidents (a'rad) with the idea of cause and effect? Their solution was the doctrine of continuous new creation (khalq jadid), which holds that every event is composed of discrete, independent parts directly created by God. This applies to both natural and human actions, but humans 'acquire' (kasb) their actions, giving a role to human will. Thus the Ash'aris built a coherent system integrating metaphysics, physics, and ethics. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, denied causal necessity in nature but insisted on human free will, leading to inconsistency in their explanations of divine power and the natural world. The author then focuses on the Ash'ari thinker al-Baqillani, who affirmed causality in a general sense as originating from divine power, but distinguished his view from those who posit nature as an independent cause. Baqillani argues against the 'nature' theory: if nature is non-existent, it cannot produce effects; if existent, it is either eternal or originated. If eternal, its effects would be eternal (like fire burning), which contradicts the consensus that events are originated. This serves as a refutation of the claim that the world arises from an eternal nature. Finally, an objection is raised: does not al-Baqillani's own position imply that God's eternal power necessitates creation from eternity? The author presents al-Baqillani's first response: he does not claim that eternal power requires simultaneous actions; rather, God can originate actions at a time of His choosing. The passage ends mid-argument, indicating a second response to come on the following page.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine of continuous new creation provides a systematic alternative to Aristotelian causality by denying necessary connection between events and attributing all events directly to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Baqillani's Conditional Argument Against Nature as an Eternal Cause
Al-Baqillani refutes the theory that nature is an efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Baqillani's conditional argument against the theory that nature is an eternal cause and evaluate its validity using modus tollens.

Core Idea
If nature were eternal, its effects would be eternal; since events are temporal, nature cannot be eternal.

Al-Baqillani, a leading Ash'ari theologian, refined the critique of causality by targeting the view that 'nature' (ṭabīʿah) is an independent efficient cause. He presents a dilemma: either nature is non-existent and cannot act, or it exists. If it exists and is eternal, its effects must be eternal—contradicting the fact that events are originated. This lesson walks through his logical argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The target is the claim that the world is produced by an eternal nature (e.g., the nature of fire, or a primordial nature).
2
Set up the dilemma
Al-Baqillani poses a disjunction: the alleged nature is either non-existent or existent. If non-existent, it cannot cause anything. So we focus on the existent branch.
3
Distinguish the subcases for an existent nature
If nature is existent, it must be either eternal or originated. He argues against the eternal case first.
4
Apply modus tollens to the eternal case
If nature is eternal, then its effects must be eternal (like fire always burning). But we know events (the world) are temporally originated, not eternal. Therefore, nature cannot be eternal.
This is a conditional argument: If P (nature eternal), then Q (effects eternal). Not Q (effects not eternal). Therefore not P.
5
Consider the originated case (incomplete in text)
The text hints that if nature is originated, it would require another cause, leading to an infinite regress or dependence on a first cause. This supports his overall refutation.
The passage ends before completing this subcase, but the logic is implied.

المسلمين منذ نشأة الفكر الإسلامي من العلية بمعناها الأرسططاليسي . وأول ما يقابلنا في هذه النشأة ، هو موقف القرآن نفسه . ومن الواضح أن القرآن في مواضع عديدة ينكر العلية ، ويتحدى « الأسباب » ويفكر « الصعود » إليها ، هذا الموقف القرآني تجاه الأسباب ، أطلق مشيخة الأشاعرة في تدعيم إنكارها ، والقضاء على تصورها الميتافيزيقي . تساءل الأشاعرة : كيف يمكن التوفيق بين اعتناقهم لفكرة الجوهر ، وتصورهم لعدم بقاء الأعراض وقتين وبين فكرة العلية ، وأن لكل سبب مسبباً في ذاته . إن فكرة الخلق الجديد المستمر ، وهي النتيجة الحتمية لتصور أن الأعراض لا تبقى زمانين ـ تعني أن كل حادث ينقسم إلى أجزاء منفصلة مستقل بعضها عن بعض تمام الإستقلال ، ولا تتصل هذه الأجزاء المنفصلة بعضها ببعض اتصال علة بمعلول أو سبب بمسبب . إن هذه الأجزاء يخلقها الله دائماً ، سواء كانت سواء أكانت فعلاً إنسانياً أو فعلاً طبيعياً . فعالم الطبيعة وعالم الإنسان محكومان بهذه الأجزاء ، غير أن عالم الإنسان يختلف عند الأشاعرة عن عالم الطبيعة كثيراً ، فبينما لا يشعر هذا العالم الطبيعي بالاختيار ، يشعر الثاني بأنه يكتسب هذه الأجزاء التي يفعلها الله فيه أو يخلقها فيه . فالإرادة الإنسانية إذن مؤثرة بخلق الله فيها للأجزاء وهكذا أقام الأشاعرة والأوائل نظاماً بديعاً للعلية متماسكاً ومتكاملاً . يستند على بحث ميتافيزيقي وطبيعي وأخلاقي .
أما المعتزلة ـ من أمثال أبي الهذيل العلاف وأبي هاشم الجبائي وغيرهما ، فقد أنكروا بوضوح العلية الأرسططاليسية وتصورها في مجال العالم الطبيعي ، ولكنهم وقد نادوا بحرية الإرادة الإنسانية ، وأن الإنسان هو الفاعل على الحقيقة ، فلم يسيروا بتصور العلية الجديد حتى نهايته . فجاءت نتائج مذهبهم في تفسير القدرة الإلهية والعالم الطبيعي مشوشة مضطربة .
سار الأشاعرة بمباحثهم في العلية قدماً ، فأنتجوا لنا نظرية العادة وجريانها على أكمل صورة لفكرة العلية الإسلامية إنما نجدها لدى مفكر الأشاعرة الكبير أبي بكر الباقلاني ( المتوفي عام ٤٠٣ هـ ) فقد اعترف الباقلاني « بالعلية بمعناها العام المطلق » واعترف بأن القدرة الإلهية هي علة وجود العالم ولكن بشكل خاص يختلف اختلافاً عن القائلين بأن « صانع العالم طبيعة من الطبائع وجب حدوث العالم عن وجودها » لأن هذه الطبيعة القديمة التي يذهب إليها القائلون بفعل الطبائع لا تخلو أن تكون « معنى موجوداً » أو معدوماً ليس بشيء » فإن كانت « معنى معدوماً ليس بشيء » لم يجز أن تفعل شيئاً ، أو أن يكون عنها أو ينسب إليها شيء « لأنه لو جاز ذلك ، جاز وجود الحوادث من كل معدوم وعن كل معدوم » وذلك باطل باتفاق . أما إن كانت الطبيعة التي ينسب إليها حدوث العالم ويتعلق حدوث العالم بها معنى موجوداً ، لم تخل تلك الطبيعة من أن تكون قديمة أو محدثة ، فان كانت قديمة ، وجب أن تكون الحوادث الكائنة عنها قديمة مثلها ، « لأن الطبيعة لم تزل موجودة ولا مانع من وجود الحوادث الموجبة عنها ، فيجب وجود الحوادث الموجبة عنها ، فيجب وجودها مع الطبيعة في القدم ، كما يجب اعتماد الحجر مع وجود طبعه ، وإحراق النار وانحلال الطبع والإسكار مع وجود طبع النار والسقمونيا والشراب ، إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع ، فكذلك يجب وجود العالم في القدم ، وإن كان محدثاً ، مع وجود الطبع الكائن عندهم » إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع (۱) ولكن هناك استحالة متفق عليها بين مختلف العقلاء - على إستحالة قدم الحوادث ، وهذا دليل على أنها لا تجوز أن تكون حادثة عن طبيعة قديمة .
ولكن ألا يلزم هذا الباقلاني - في قوله هو والأشاعرة - إن صانع العالم لم يزل قادراً على ايجاده ، لأن قدرته على الإيجاد قديمة . ويرد الباقلاني على هذا بأن هذا لا يجب من وجهين :
أولاً : يقرر الباقلاني أنه لا يقول بأن الله قادر بقدرة في الأزل على أن تكون الأفعال مع القدرة ، وإنما هو يذهب إلى أن الله قادر على « أن يستأنف الأفعال وعلى أن يحدثها في زمان كانت قبله معدومة ، ومحال أن تكون قدرة
Muslims since the beginning of Islamic thought [rejected] causality in its Aristotelian sense. The first thing we encounter in this beginning is the stance of the Quran itself. It is clear that the Quran in many places denies causality, challenges 'causes', and considers 'ascending' to them. This Quranic stance towards causes liberated the Ash'ari masters to strengthen its denial and eliminate its metaphysical conception. The Ash'aris asked: How can we reconcile their adherence to the idea of substance, and their conception of the non-continuance of accidents over two moments, with the idea of causality, that every cause has an effect in itself? The idea of continuous new creation, which is the inevitable result of the conception that accidents do not remain for two moments, means that every event is divided into separate parts, each completely independent of the other, and these separate parts are not connected to each other by the connection of cause to effect or reason to result. These parts are always created by God, whether they are a human action or a natural action. The world of nature and the world of man are governed by these parts, but the world of man differs according to the Ash'aris greatly from the world of nature. While this natural world does not feel choice, the second feels that it acquires these parts that God does in it or creates in it. Human will therefore is effective by God's creation of parts in it. Thus the Ash'aris and the early ones established a wonderful system of causality, coherent and integrated, based on metaphysical, natural, and ethical inquiry. As for the Mu'tazila—like Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf and Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i and others—they clearly denied Aristotelian causality and its conception in the natural world, but since they advocated for human free will and that man is the true agent, they did not follow the new conception of causality to its end. So the results of their school in explaining divine power and the natural world came out confused and disturbed. The Ash'aris advanced their investigations into causality, producing for us the theory of habit (al-'adah). Its most perfect form of the Islamic idea of causality is found with the great Ash'ari thinker Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403 AH). Al-Baqillani acknowledged 'causality in its general absolute sense' and acknowledged that divine power is the cause of the existence of the world, but in a specific way different from those who say 'the maker of the world is a nature among natures, and the occurrence of the world is necessitated by its existence'. For this eternal nature that the proponents of the action of natures hold to must either be 'an existing meaning' or 'non-existent, not a thing'. If it is 'a non-existent meaning, not a thing', it cannot do anything, nor can anything come from it or be attributed to it, 'because if that were permissible, the occurrence of events from every non-existent and from every non-existent would be permissible', and that is false by consensus. If the nature to which the occurrence of the world is attributed and upon which the occurrence of the world depends is an existing meaning, then that nature must be either eternal or originated. If it is eternal, then the events coming from it must be eternal like it, 'because the nature has always existed and there is no obstacle to the occurrence of the events necessitated by it, so the occurrence of the events necessitated by it is necessary, so their existence with the nature in eternity is necessary, just as the downward motion of a stone is necessary given its nature, and the burning of fire and dissolution of a temperament and intoxication given the nature of fire, scammony, and wine, if there is no obstacle preventing that. Similarly, the existence of the world in eternity would be necessary, and if it is originated, [it would require] the existence of the nature as they claim' if there is no obstacle preventing that. But there is an agreed-upon impossibility among various rational minds—the impossibility of the eternity of events. This is proof that they cannot be originated from an eternal nature. But does this not obligate al-Baqillani—in his statement and the Ash'aris—that the maker of the world has always been able to bring it into existence, because his power to bring into existence is eternal? Al-Baqillani responds to this that this is not necessary for two reasons: First: al-Baqillani affirms that he does not say that God is powerful with an eternal power such that actions are concomitant with power, but rather he holds that God is powerful 'to initiate actions and to bring them into being in a time in which they were previously non-existent, and it is impossible for power [to be]...'
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the Islamic theological schools' engagement with the Aristotelian concept of causality. The author begins by noting the Quran's rejection of causality, which set the stage for the Ash'ari school to deny the metaphysical necessity of causes. The Ash'aris faced an internal tension: how to reconcile their acceptance of substance (jawhar) and the non-continuance of accidents (a'rad) with the idea of cause and effect? Their solution was the doctrine of continuous new creation (khalq jadid), which holds that every event is composed of discrete, independent parts directly created by God. This applies to both natural and human actions, but humans 'acquire' (kasb) their actions, giving a role to human will. Thus the Ash'aris built a coherent system integrating metaphysics, physics, and ethics. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, denied causal necessity in nature but insisted on human free will, leading to inconsistency in their explanations of divine power and the natural world. The author then focuses on the Ash'ari thinker al-Baqillani, who affirmed causality in a general sense as originating from divine power, but distinguished his view from those who posit nature as an independent cause. Baqillani argues against the 'nature' theory: if nature is non-existent, it cannot produce effects; if existent, it is either eternal or originated. If eternal, its effects would be eternal (like fire burning), which contradicts the consensus that events are originated. This serves as a refutation of the claim that the world arises from an eternal nature. Finally, an objection is raised: does not al-Baqillani's own position imply that God's eternal power necessitates creation from eternity? The author presents al-Baqillani's first response: he does not claim that eternal power requires simultaneous actions; rather, God can originate actions at a time of His choosing. The passage ends mid-argument, indicating a second response to come on the following page.

What is the status of events (al-ḥawādith) according to al-Baqillani's argument?
A) They are eternal because they come from an eternal nature.
B) They are temporally originated (muhdatha) – not eternal.
C) They are both eternal and originated.
D) They are illusions with no real existence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani's modus tollens argument effectively refutes the claim that the world arises from an eternal nature by showing that such a nature would necessitate eternal events, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional argument (modus tollens) on an Islamic topic: for example, 'If the Quran were created, it would have originated in time; but the Quran is not created (it is eternal); therefore...' Complete the argument and check validity.
?
Identify the conditional premises in al-Baqillani's argument. Write them as If P then Q, and Not Q, therefore Not P.

Refuting Natural Causation: Al-Baqillani's Critique
Al-Baqillani critiques natural causation and defends divine volitional power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Baqillani's argument against necessary causal connection and explain why divine power does not necessitate its effects.

Core Idea
Al-Baqillani denies necessary causation to preserve divine power and will.

In Islamic theology, the Ash'ari scholar al-Baqillani launched a profound critique against the philosophers' doctrine that natures (like fire) necessarily produce their effects. This passage presents his logical dismantling of that view. By working through his argument, you will understand a key moment in the kalam debate on causality and its implications for divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
What exact claim is al-Baqillani attacking? It is the statement that 'the Maker of the world is a nature among the natures, and the occurrence of the world from its existence is necessary.' Understand that this implies an eternal, self-sufficient cause that necessitates the world.
2
Extract the disjunctive argument
Al-Baqillani splits the nature into two possibilities: it is either nonexistent or existent. If nonexistent, nothing can come from it—absurd. If existent, it must be either eternal or originated. This sets up a logical fork.
Notice how the argument uses a complete disjunction (either-or) to cover all possibilities.
3
Analyze the conditional on eternity
Assume the nature is eternal. Then its effect (the world) would also have to be eternal, just as a stone sinks necessarily with its nature. But the eternity of events is impossible by consensus. Therefore, the world cannot be caused by an eternal nature.
This is a conditional argument: if eternal nature → eternal world, but eternal world is impossible → no eternal nature.
4
Examine al-Baqillani's objection response
An opponent might say: 'You Ash'aris also believe God is eternally powerful; doesn't that also require the world to be eternal?' Al-Baqillani responds in two ways: (1) God's power in eternity does not demand simultaneous effects; He can delay actions. (2) Divine power is not a natural cause that necessitates; it is free.
This shows the subtlety: al-Baqillani distinguishes his view from the philosophers' notion of necessary causation.
5
Conclude: denial of necessary connection
The entire critique aims to deny the necessary connection (talazum daruri) between cause and effect. For al-Baqillani, there is no natural necessity; every event depends directly on God's will and power.
This is the foundation of the Ash'ari occasionalist position.

Source Text

العالم ولكن بشكل خاص يختلف اختلافاً عن القائلين بأن « صانع العالم طبيعة من الطبائع وجب حدوث العالم عن وجودها » لأن هذه الطبيعة القديمة التي يذهب إليها القائلون بفعل الطبائع لا تخلو أن تكون « معنى موجوداً » أو معدوماً ليس بشيء » فإن كانت « معنى معدوماً ليس بشيء » لم يجز أن تفعل شيئاً ، أو أن يكون عنها أو ينسب إليها شيء « لأنه لو جاز ذلك ، جاز وجود الحوادث من كل معدوم وعن كل معدوم » وذلك باطل باتفاق . أما إن كانت الطبيعة التي ينسب إليها حدوث العالم ويتعلق حدوث العالم بها معنى موجوداً ، لم تخل تلك الطبيعة من أن تكون قديمة أو محدثة ، فان كانت قديمة ، وجب أن تكون الحوادث الكائنة عنها قديمة مثلها ، « لأن الطبيعة لم تزل موجودة ولا مانع من وجود الحوادث الموجبة عنها ، فيجب وجود الحوادث الموجبة عنها ، فيجب وجودها مع الطبيعة في القدم ، كما يجب اعتماد الحجر مع وجود طبعه ، وإحراق النار وانحلال الطبع والإسكار مع وجود طبع النار والسقمونيا والشراب ، إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع ، فكذلك يجب وجود العالم في القدم ، وإن كان محدثاً ، مع وجود الطبع الكائن عندهم » إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع (۱) ولكن هناك استحالة متفق عليها بين مختلف العقلاء - على إستحالة قدم الحوادث ، وهذا دليل على أنها لا تجوز أن تكون حادثة عن طبيعة قديمة .

ولكن ألا يلزم هذا الباقلاني - في قوله هو والأشاعرة - إن صانع العالم لم يزل قادراً على ايجاده ، لأن قدرته على الإيجاد قديمة . ويرد الباقلاني على هذا بأن هذا لا يجب من وجهين :

أولاً : يقرر الباقلاني أنه لا يقول بأن الله قادر بقدرة في الأزل على أن تكون الأفعال مع القدرة ، وإنما هو يذهب إلى أن الله قادر على « أن يستأنف الأفعال وعلى أن يحدثها في زمان كانت قبله معدومة ، ومحال أن تكون قدرة (۱) الباقلاني : التمهيد ص ٥٢ .
على ما لم يكن معدوماً قبل وجوده ، فلم يجب قدم الأفعال لقدم القدرة عليها(۱) .

ثانياً : يقرر الباقلاني أن القدرة الإلهية ليست هي إطلاق العلة الطبيعية فليست هي علة للأفعال ، ولا موجبة لها ، حسب ما يقوله الفلاسفة . في إيجاب الطبع لحدث ما يحدث عنه ، وكونه علة له ، ووجوب كونه عنه . بل إن الباقلاني يذهب إلى أنه من الممكن أن توجد قدرة الله في الأزل ، والله غير فاعل بها ، وإن كان يصح أن يفعل بها ، فقدرة الله إذن ليست بعلة ولا سبب لمقدوره ، ولا موجبة له (۲) .

وكذلك القول في الإرادة ، إنها قديمة ، ولكنها ارادة لكون الفعل على التراخي ، ولأنها ليست علة لوجود المراد .

ثم يناقش الباقلاني الافتراض الآخر عن هذه الطبيعة وهي كونها محدثة ويبين تهافت هذا الافتراض . ثم يهاجم القائلين بأن العالم بأسره مركب من الطبائع الأربعة القديمة الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ، ويرى أنها أعراض محدثة متضادة على الأجسام ، وأن الأعراض لا يجوز أن تفعل شيئاً (۳) .

أراد الباقلاني من كل هذه الإلزامات والإنتقادات التي وجهها إلى القائلين بفعل الطباع . والقائلين بقدم العالم إنكار الأساس الذي تقوم عليه هذه الأبحاث وهو التلازم الضروري بين العلة والمعلول ، لكي يثبت قدرة الله شاملة لكل شيء ، وإرادته خالقة لكل شيء .
The world, but specifically it differs in difference from those who say that 'the Maker of the world is a nature among the natures, and the occurrence of the world from its existence is necessary', because this ancient nature to which the proponents of natural actions go cannot but be either 'an existing meaning' or a nonexistent nothing. If it were a nonexistent nothing, it would not be possible for it to do anything, or for anything to be from it or attributed to it, 'because if that were permissible, the existence of events from every nonexistent and by every nonexistent would be permissible', and that is false by consensus. But if the nature to which the occurrence of the world is attributed and to which the occurrence of the world is connected is an existing meaning, that nature cannot but be either eternal or originated. If it were eternal, then the events occurring from it would necessarily be eternal like it, 'because nature has never ceased to exist and there is no impediment to the existence of the events necessitated by it, so the existence of the events necessitated by it is necessary, so their existence is necessary with nature in eternity, just as the dependent existence of a stone is necessary with the existence of its nature, and the burning of fire and dissolution of nature and intoxication with the existence of the nature of fire, scammony, and wine, if no impediment prevents that, so likewise the existence of the world in eternity is necessary, although it is originated, with the existence of the nature according to them' if no impediment prevents that. But there is an impossibility agreed upon by diverse rational people regarding the impossibility of the eternity of events, and this is evidence that they cannot be originated from an eternal nature.

But does this not necessitate for al-Baqillani - in his saying, he and the Ash'arites - that the Maker of the world has never ceased to be able to bring it into existence, because His power to bring into existence is eternal? And al-Baqillani responds to this that this is not necessary for two reasons:

First: al-Baqillani states that he does not say that God is able with a power in eternity that actions exist with the power, but rather he holds that God is able to 'initiate actions and to bring them about in a time in which they were previously nonexistent, and it is impossible that a power (1) al-Baqillani: al-Tamhid, p. 52.
exist over what was not nonexistent before its existence, so the eternity of actions is not necessitated by the eternity of power over them(1).'

Second: al-Baqillani states that divine power is not the same as absolute natural causality; it is neither a cause for actions nor a necessitator of them, according to what the philosophers say regarding the necessitation of nature for the occurrence that occurs from it, and its being a cause for it, and the necessity of its coming from it. Rather, al-Baqillani holds that it is possible that God's power exists in eternity while God is not acting through it, even though He may validly act through it, so God's power is thus neither a cause nor a ground for what is within its capability, nor a necessitator of it(2).

And likewise concerning will: it is eternal, but it is a will for the action to be with delay, and because it is not a cause for the existence of the object of will.

Then al-Baqillani discusses the other assumption about this nature, namely that it is originated, and shows the incoherence of this assumption. Then he attacks those who say that the entire world is composed of the four ancient natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, and he holds that they are originated, contrary accidents upon bodies, and that accidents cannot do anything(3).

Through all these objections and criticisms that he directed against the proponents of natural actions and the proponents of the eternity of the world, al-Baqillani intended to deny the foundation upon which these investigations rest, namely the necessary connection between cause and effect, in order to establish that the power of God encompasses everything and His will creates everything.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents al-Baqillani's critical refutation of the doctrine of natural causation (فعل الطباع). The author first explains al-Baqillani's argument against those who claim the world's maker is an eternal nature. The argument proceeds disjunctively: either that nature is nonexistent or existent. If nonexistent, nothing can come from it, which is absurd. If existent, then it is either eternal or originated. If eternal, then its effects must also be eternal, just as a stone's sinking is necessary with its nature. But the eternity of events is impossible by consensus, so the world cannot be caused by an eternal nature. The author then anticipates an objection: does this imply that al-Baqillani's own view—that God is eternally powerful—also necessitates the eternity of the world? Al-Baqillani's response is twofold. First, he distinguishes his position: God's power in eternity does not require that actions exist simultaneously; rather, He can create actions in time after they were nonexistent. Second, he asserts that divine power is not like a natural cause that necessarily produces its effect; it does not necessitate or cause the effect in the way nature does. Similarly, the divine will is eternal but intends action with delay. Al-Baqillani then attacks the alternative assumption that the nature is originated, finding it incoherent, and rejects the theory of four ancient elements, arguing they are accidents that cannot cause anything. The overall goal is to deny the necessary connection between cause and effect (التلازم الضروري), affirming God's absolute power and will. Throughout, the author uses logical reasoning: conditional arguments ('if eternal, then... impossible'), disjunction (either nonexistent or existent), and analogies (stone sinking, fire burning). All examples are from the Islamic theological tradition.

Key Terms
طبيعة (tabi'a) - nature or inherent characteristic claimed to necessarily produce effects
علة (illa) - cause that necessitates its effect
قدرة (qudra) - divine power
التلازم الضروري (al-talazum al-daruri) - necessary connection between cause and effect

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani's argument, why can the world not be caused by an eternal nature?
A) Because an eternal nature would require the world to be eternal, which is impossible.
B) Because an eternal nature cannot be a 'meaning' at all.
C) Because divine power is not eternal.
D) Because the world is composed of four elements.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani refutes natural causation by denying the necessary connection between cause and effect, affirming instead that every event depends directly on God's will and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct al-Baqillani's disjunctive argument in your own words. Identify the key premise and conclusion, and explain how it refutes the claim that the world is caused by an eternal nature.
?
Compare al-Baqillani's view on divine power with the philosophers' concept of natural causation. What is the logical difference regarding necessity? (Use at most 5 sentences.)

Ghazali's Denial of Necessary Causation: Al-'Illiyya and Al-'Adah
Presentation of Ghazali's critique and his defense against the objection of absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Ghazali's distinction between necessary causation and habitual custom and state why he denies necessary causal connections.

Core Idea
Ghazali replaces necessary causes with habitual custom to defend miracles without abandoning reason.

This lesson introduces one of the most controversial critiques in Islamic thought: Imam al-Ghazali's denial of necessary causation. He argues that what we call causation is merely constant conjunction based on God's habit. This radical shift has profound implications for logic, especially for how we define things and how we reason from the seen to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Locate the principle under attack
Find where the author says 'All investigations of logic collapse with the collapse of this principle.' This shows that the principle of necessary causation is the target.
The principle is 'al-'illiyya' (causal necessity) from Aristotelian logic.
2
Identify the objection
The opponent objects that denying necessary causation leads to absurd scenarios: beasts, fires, enemies you don't see; a book turning into an animal; a boy into a dog. These are considered 'ugly consequences' (majalat shani'a).
Al-Ghazali anticipates this objection.
3
Extract al-Ghazali's response
Al-Ghazali replies that such possibilities are possible but never actual. We rely on 'habitual custom' (al-'adah) that establishes in our minds the expectation of regularity.
The key phrase: 'istimrar al-'adah biha marratan ba'da ukhra yurassikh fi adhhanina...'
4
Contrast necessity and habit
Necessity implies that the effect must follow; habit implies only that it usually follows. Habit does not provide logical certainty but practical certainty based on repeated observation.
This distinction allows for miracles without denying observed regularities.
5
Assess the logical impact
If causation is not necessary, then definitions based on essential properties become problematic, and demonstrations (burhan) that rely on causal chains lose their force. This is why logic 'collapses'.
The author mentions that this leads the Usuliyyun to oppose Aristotelians on definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتتداعى أبحاث المنطق كلها بتداعي هذا القانون . فإنه دخل في نظرية البرهان كما دخل في نظرية الحد وأدى بطلانه في تلك النظرية الأخيرة إلى مخالفة الأصوليين للأرسططاليسيين في أغلب مباحث الحد . بل نرى إمام الحرمين يقول « إن الجمع بالعلة - في قياس الغائب على الشاهد لا أصل له إذ لا علة ولا معلول عندنا » . تنبه الغزالي إلى أنه سيعترض عليه بأن إنكار العلية يؤدي إلى مجالات شنيعة فإن من أنكر لزوم المعلولات عن عللها . وأضافها إلى إرادة مخترعها ، ولم يكن لهذه الإرادة أيضاً منهج مخصوص متعين ، بل يمكن تعينه وتنوعه فجاز لكل واحد منا أن يكون بين يديه سباع ضارية ونيران مشتعلة وجبال راسية وأعداء مستعدة بالأسلحة لقتله وهو لا يراها . . . ومن وضع كتاباً في بيته فيجوز أن يكون قد انقلب حيواناً ، أو لو ترك غلاماً في بيته ، فيجوز انقلابه كلباً ، أو ترك التراب ، فيجوز انقلابه مسكاً . ولكن يجيب الغزالي : بأن هذه الأشياء التي نتجت عن القضاء على قانون العلية لم تحدث وليست هي واجبة . ولكن لا شك أنها ممكنة ، يجوز أن تقع وألا تقع « واستمرار العادة بها مرة بعد أخرى ترسخ في أذهاننا جريانها على وفق العادة الماضية ، ترسخاً لا تنفك عنه » . إن من المسلم به أن النار خلقت ، بحيث إذا تلاقت مع قطعتين متماثلتين أحرقتهما ، ولم تفرق بينهما طالما كانتا متماثلتين من جميع الوجوه ولكن مع هذا يجوز أن يلقى شخص في النار فلا يحترق ، فقد تتغير صفة النار أو تتغير صفة الشخص . يتبين لنا هذا الدافع الحقيقي لنقد العلية عند الغزالي . إن تطبيق هذا المبدأ في السمعيات سيحيل تحقيق المعجزات « لم يتخلق قط من نطفة الإنسان إلا إنسان ومن نطفة الفرس إلا فرس ، من حيث إن حصوله من الفرس أوجب ترجيحاً لمناسبة صورة الفرس على سائر الصور ، فلم يقبل إلا الصورة المرجحة بهذا الطريق وكذلك لم ينبت من الشعير قط حنطة ولا من بذر الكمثرى تفاح ثم رأينا أجناساً من الحيوانات تتولد من التراب ولا تتوالد قط كالديدان ، ومنها ما يتولد ويتوالد جميعاً كالفأر والحية والعقرب وكان تولدها من التراب ويختلف استعدادها لقبول الصور بأمور غابت عنا . . . ومن استقرأ عجائب العالم ، لم يستبعد من قدرة الله ما يحكى من معجزات الأنبياء » . رأى الغزالي التعارض بين مبادىء المنطق اليوناني وبين النقل ، فلم ير بداً من القضاء على الأول لكي ينقذ الثاني . وقد تابع الغزالي مفكر عاصره ومات بعده بقليل الشهرستاني ( المتوفي عام ٥٤٨ ) ، فربط أيضاً نقد العلية ، وخرق العادة بمعجزات الأنبياء ثم عرض الأشعري المتأخر ابن خلدون لنظرية الأشعرية في العلية عرضاً فاتناً في مقدمته .
All investigations of logic collapse with the collapse of this principle. For it entered into the theory of demonstration as it entered into the theory of definition, and its invalidity in that latter theory led the Usuliyyun to oppose the Aristotelians in most topics of definition. Indeed, we see Imam al-Haramayn saying: 'Combination by cause—in reasoning from the unseen to the seen—has no basis, for there is no cause nor effect with us.' Al-Ghazali realized that he would be objected to that the denial of causality leads to ugly consequences, for whoever denies the necessity of effects from their causes and attributes them to the will of their Creator, and that will also has no specific determined method, but rather can be determined and varied—then it would be possible for each of us to have in front of him ferocious beasts, blazing fires, solid mountains, and enemies armed to kill him while he does not see them... And whoever places a book in his house, it could have turned into an animal, or if he left a boy in his house, he could have turned into a dog, or if he left dust, it could have turned into musk. But al-Ghazali replies: That these things that resulted from nullifying the law of causality have not occurred and are not necessary. But there is no doubt they are possible, they may occur or not occur: 'The continuation of habit with them time after time establishes in our minds their occurrence according to past habit, an establishment from which we cannot break free.' It is accepted that fire was created such that if it meets two similar pieces it burns them, and it does not differentiate between them as long as they are similar in all respects, but with this it is possible that a person is thrown into the fire and does not burn, for the property of the fire may change or the property of the person may change. This shows us the true motive for al-Ghazali's critique of causality. Applying this principle to audible matters would make the occurrence of miracles impossible: 'Never was anything other than a human created from human sperm, nor from horse sperm except a horse, because its occurrence from the horse necessitated a weighting for the suitability of the horse's form over other forms, so it accepted only the form weighted in this way. Likewise, barley never grew wheat, nor from a pear seed an apple. Then we saw species of animals generated from dust without ever reproducing, like worms, and some that both generate and reproduce, like the mouse, snake, and scorpion, and their generation from dust varies in their readiness to accept forms due to matters hidden from us... Whoever examines the wonders of the world will not consider remote from God's power what is narrated of the miracles of the prophets.' Al-Ghazali saw the contradiction between the principles of Greek logic and transmitted tradition, so he saw no alternative but to destroy the former in order to save the latter. Al-Ghazali was followed by a contemporary thinker who died shortly after him, al-Shahrastani (d. 548 AH), who also linked the critique of causality and the breach of habit to the miracles of the prophets. Then the later Ash'ari Ibn Khaldun presented the Ash'ari theory of causality in a fascinating manner in his Muqaddimah.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Imam al-Ghazali's (d. 505 AH) critique of Aristotelian causality and its implications for logic. The author begins by stating that all logical investigations are undermined by the collapse of the principle of necessary causation ('illiyya). He notes that this principle had entered both the theory of demonstration (burhan) and definition (hadd), and its invalidation in the latter led the Usuliyyun (scholars of legal theory) to oppose the Aristotelians. He quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni's rejection of analogical reasoning by cause (jama' bil-'illa) in arguments from the unseen to the seen. The author then presents the objection that al-Ghazali anticipated: denying necessary causation leads to absurd consequences—one could doubt the existence of beasts, fires, or armed enemies before oneself, or believe a book could turn into an animal or a boy into a dog. Al-Ghazali's response is crucial: these possibilities are not actual; they are merely possible. They can occur or not, but habitual custom (al-'adah) repeatedly observed establishes in our minds a firm expectation of regularity, for example, that fire burns similar objects. However, it remains possible that the fire's property or the person's property could change, allowing a miracle. The author explains that this motive is to preserve the possibility of miracles in the audibles (sam'iyyat). Al-Ghazali argues that the normal course of nature (e.g., human semen produces only humans, barley never wheat) does not preclude that God can break this habit, as seen in cases like animals generated from dust. Thus, he saw a contradiction between Greek logical principles and revealed tradition, and chose to undermine the former to safeguard the latter. The author notes that al-Shahrastani and Ibn Khaldun followed this Ash'ari critique of causality.

Key Terms
العلية
العادة
الجمع بالعلة
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is al-Ghazali's response to the objection that denying causality leads to absurd possibilities?
A) He accepts that such absurdities are actual and unavoidable.
B) He argues that such possibilities are possible but never actual, and we rely on habitual custom.
C) He rejects the objection outright without proof.
D) He claims that causality is necessary for miracles.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali denies necessary causation, asserting that observed regularities are due to God's habit, which can be broken, thereby preserving the possibility of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a natural regularity mentioned in the Qur'an (e.g., water extinguishing fire) and explain how an Ash'ari would interpret it using al-'adah.
?
Contrast Ghazali's position with that of Ibn Sina on the necessity of causal relations, using examples from their writings.

The Impact of Ghazali's Critique on Logic and Legal Theory
Explanation of the implications and motive behind Ghazali's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Ghazali's denial of necessary causation undermines Aristotelian theories of definition (hadd) and demonstration (burhan) and how this affected Islamic legal theory.

Core Idea
Denying causal necessity collapses Aristotelian logic; Ghazali chooses revelation over Greek rationalism.

In this passage, we see the far-reaching effects of Ghazali's critique. Not only does it challenge the theory of demonstration, but it also enters the theory of definition, leading the scholars of usul al-fiqh to oppose the Aristotelians. Moreover, Ghazali's motive becomes clear: to defend the miracles of the prophets against philosophical objections. This lesson traces those consequences.

Guided Path
1
Find the two affected theories
The passage says causality 'entered into the theory of demonstration as it entered into the theory of definition.' Its invalidity in definition led the Usuliyyun to oppose the Aristotelians on most topics of definition.
Theory of demonstration (burhan) and theory of definition (hadd) are foundational to Aristotelian logic.
2
Examine the quote from Imam al-Haramayn
Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni rejects 'combination by cause' (al-jam' bil-'illa) in reasoning from the unseen to the seen (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid), because there is no cause or effect.
This directly impacts legal analogy (qiyas) used in Islamic jurisprudence.
3
Understand the motive
The passage explicitly says that applying the principle of necessary causation to 'audible matters' (sam'iyyat) would make the occurrence of miracles impossible. Ghazali wants to prevent that.
Sam'iyyat refers to theological truths known from revelation, like miracles and the afterlife.
4
Read the examples of natural diversity
Al-Ghazali gives examples of animals generated from dust (e.g., worms) and plants that do not follow strict species lines, showing that nature is not fixed; God's power can produce variety.
These examples weaken the claim that causal chains are necessary and uniform.
5
Connect to later thinkers
The author notes that al-Shahrastani and Ibn Khaldun followed Ghazali's line. Ibn Khaldun presented the Ash'ari theory of causality in his Muqaddimah.
This shows the lasting impact of Ghazali's critique on Islamic thought.

Al-Ghazali's Critique of Causality: Miracles and the Concept of 'Ada
Al-Ghazali's real motive for critiquing causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Ghazali's argument against necessary causality and explain how it defends prophetic miracles using the concept of 'ada (habit).

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali sacrificed Aristotelian logic to defend miracles by denying necessary connection between cause and effect.

Al-Ghazali observed a conflict between the necessity required by Greek logic and the possibility of miracles in revelation. He argued that observed regularities are not necessary but are God's habit, which can be broken. This lesson examines his reasoning and its implications for Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ghazali's motive
What does al-Ghazali aim to defend? Find in the passage where he states that applying the principle of causality would negate miracles.
He sees a direct conflict between logical necessity and prophetic miracles.
2
Extract the principle he critiques
The necessary connection between cause and effect. Notice how he uses examples like human/horse reproduction and plant growth to show that outcomes are not necessary but vary.
3
Analyze the examples
List the examples: human sperm always produces human, horse sperm always horse, wheat never from barley, etc. What do they demonstrate? They show regularity, but al-Ghazali argues this is habit, not necessity.
He also mentions animals generated from dust without breeding.
4
Understand the alternative: 'Ada
Al-Ghazali introduces the concept of 'ada (habit) to explain regularities without necessitating them. This allows for kharq al-ada (breaking of habit) in miracles.
Habit is God's usual pattern, not a logical requirement.
5
Connect to kharq al-ada
How does this allow miracles? Since there is no necessary connection, God can break the habit, making miracles possible without contradiction.
This is the core of the Ash'ari occasionalist position.

Source Text
