يتبين لنا هذا الدافع الحقيقي لنقد العلية عند الغزالي. إن تطبيق هذا المبدأ في السمعيات سيحيل تحقيق المعجزات «لم يتخلق قط من نطفة الإنسان إلا إنسان ومن نطفة الفرس إلا فرس، من حيث إن حصوله من الفرس أوجب ترجيحاً لمناسبة صورة الفرس على سائر الصور، فلم يقبل إلا الصورة المرجحة بهذا الطريق وكذلك لم ينبت من الشعير قط حنطة ولا من بذر الكمثرى تفاح ثم رأينا أجناساً من الحيوانات تتولد من التراب ولا تتوالد قط كالديدان، ومنها ما يتولد ويتوالد جميعاً كالفأر والحية والعقرب وكان تولدها من التراب ويختلف استعدادها لقبول الصور بأمور غابت عنا... ومن استقرأ عجائب العالم، لم يستبعد من قدرة الله ما يحكى من معجزات الأنبياء». رأى الغزالي التعارض بين مبادىء المنطق اليوناني وبين النقل، فلم ير بداً من القضاء على الأول لكي ينقذ الثاني. وقد تابع الغزالي مفكر عاصره ومات بعده بقليل الشهرستاني، فربط أيضاً نقد العلية، وخرق العادة بمعجزات الأنبياء ثم عرض الأشعري المتأخر ابن خلدون لنظرية الأشعرية في العلية عرضاً فاتناً في مقدمته. موقف الأوروبيين المحدثين: سبق المسلمون ثلاثة من فلاسفة أوروبا المحدثين إلى نقد العلية بمعناها الأرسططاليسي وهم مالبرانش وبركلي وهيوم. أما مالبرانش فإنه يرى أن العلة الحقيقية هي التي يدرك العقل بينها وبين معلولها علاقة ضرورية. ولكن لا يوجد إلا الموجود الكامل كمالاً لا نهاية له الذي يدرك العقل علامة ضرورية ما بين إرادته وما بين المعلولات، فلا يوجد إذن إلا الله، وهو العلة الحقيقية. فلا اقتران ولا ارتباط ضروري، إلا بقدر ما تريد ذلك الإرادة والحرية الإلهية، قد ضحى أيضاً هذا الفيلسوف اللاهوتي بالمنطق، لكي ينقذ الإرادة الإلهية وشمولها. فأنكر «أن توجد إلا علة حقيقية، لأنه لا يوجد إلا إله حقيقي واحد» وهذه العلة ليست إلا الله ذاته، وكل العلل الطبيعية ليست عللاً حقيقية، ولكنها علل مناسبة. أما بركلي فقد نقد العلية أيضاً بأن أرجعها إلى العقل وقد مهد هو ومالبرانش الطريق لهيوم. وهيوم أهم هؤلاء الثلاثة في نقده للعلية على العموم. ثم إن نقده للعلية يشبه أيضاً نقد المسلمين لها بل إن رينان يذهب إلى أن هيوم لم يتكلم في هذه الناحية أكثر مما تكلمه الغزالي على الدوام وبإستمرار أي أنكر الضرورة التي بين العلة والمعلول، وقرر أنها ليست ضرورة منطقية، ولكنها ضرورة تجريبية فحسب، يعتمد على مجرد تتابع عادي، قائم على المشاهدة والملاحظة الحسية. فتصور العلة ليس في الحقيقة إلا تتابع العادة ومنشأ في المخيلة التي لا تستطيع في هذا الميدان أن تعمل بحرية كما تستطيع هذا العمل في ميدان الخرافات. فإذا ما رأينا دائماً فكرة تتابع أخرى، فإننا نصطلح على اعتبار الأولى علة، وننتظر أن نرى الأخرى تتبعها. وفي هذه الحالة نسميها معلولاً وإذا تكرر تلازم الحدوث وتكررت ملاحظتنا لهذا التلازم. تكونت لنا هذه العادة العقلية التي هي فقط أساس تصورنا لربط العلية. وكأن هيوم قد قرأ الغزالي، ونقل عنه فكرته نقلاً تاماً. وقد حاول ابن تيمية أن ينسب نقد العلية عند الأشاعرة وعند الهروي الأنصاري الشيخ السلفي القديم إلى جهم بن صفوان. ومن العجب أن نرى ابن تيمية هذا العدو الممتاز للمنطق الارسططاليسي كما سنرى فيما بعد، ينكر فكرة العادة التي نادى بها الأشاعرة، كما نادى بها شيخه وهو الهروي الانصاري. لقد نادى الهروي الأنصاري قبل الغزالي بفكرة العادة فقال
This shows us the real motive for al-Ghazali's critique of causality. Applying this principle in matters of revelation (sam'iyyat) would negate the realization of miracles: 'Never has anything other than a human been created from human sperm, nor anything other than a horse from horse sperm, insofar as its coming from the horse necessitated a preference for the form of the horse over other forms, so it accepted only the form preferred in this way; likewise, wheat never grew from barley, nor apples from pear seeds. Then we see kinds of animals generated from dust without breeding, like worms, and some that both generate and breed, like mice, snakes, and scorpions—their generation from dust and their varying receptivity to forms due to matters hidden from us... Whoever investigates the wonders of the world will not deem improbable, concerning the power of God, what is reported of the miracles of the prophets.' Al-Ghazali saw a conflict between the principles of Greek logic and revelation, so he saw no escape but to eliminate the former to save the latter. He was followed by his contemporary al-Shahrastani, who also linked the critique of causality and the breaking of habit (kharq al-'ada) with prophetic miracles. Later, the late Ash'ari Ibn Khaldun presented a fascinating exposition of the Ash'ari theory of causality in his Muqaddimah. The stance of modern Europeans: Muslims preceded three modern European philosophers in critiquing causality in its Aristotelian sense: Malebranche, Berkeley, and Hume. Malebranche holds that the true cause is that which the intellect perceives a necessary relation between it and its effect. But there is only the absolutely perfect Being, with whose will the intellect perceives a necessary connection to effects; thus there is no cause but God, who is the true cause. There is no necessary conjunction or connection except as the divine will and freedom determine. This theologian also sacrificed logic to save divine will and its comprehensiveness. He denied 'that there exists any true cause except one true God,' so every natural cause is not a true cause but an occasional cause (Occasionelles). Berkeley also critiqued causality by reducing it to the mind, and he and Malebranche paved the way for Hume. Hume is the most important of these three in his critique of causality in general. Moreover, his critique resembles that of the Muslims; indeed, Renan holds that Hume said nothing more in this regard than al-Ghazali repeatedly and continuously said—that is, he denied the necessity between cause and effect, asserting it is not logical necessity but merely empirical necessity, based on mere habitual succession founded on sensory observation. So the concept of cause is in reality nothing but a succession of habit, originating in the imagination, which cannot work freely in this field as it can in superstition. If we always see one idea following another, we conventionally consider the first a cause and expect to see the other follow. In this case we call it an effect, and when the conjunction of occurrence is repeated and our observation of this conjunction is repeated, this mental habit is formed, which is the only basis for our concept of causality. It is as if Hume had read al-Ghazali and fully transmitted his idea. Ibn Taymiyya attempted to attribute the critique of causality by the Ash'aris and by al-Harawi al-Ansari, the ancient Salafi shaykh, to Jahm ibn Safwan. It is astonishing to see Ibn Taymiyya, this outstanding enemy of Aristotelian logic—as we shall see later—denying the idea of 'habit' (al-'ada) championed by the Ash'aris and by his own teacher al-Harawi al-Ansari. Al-Harawi al-Ansari championed the idea of 'habit' before al-Ghazali, saying: ...
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a historical and comparative analysis of the critique of causality in Islamic and European thought. It opens by identifying al-Ghazali's real motive: to defend prophetic miracles by rejecting necessary causal connections in the natural world. The author quotes al-Ghazali's argument that observed regularities (like reproduction and growth) are not necessary but due to God's habit ('ada), which can be broken. Al-Ghazali saw a contradiction between Greek logic's necessity and revealed miracles, so he prioritized revelation. The text then notes that al-Shahrastani and Ibn Khaldun continued this critique. The second part compares the Islamic critique with that of Malebranche, Berkeley, and Hume. Malebranche denied natural causality entirely, attributing all true causality to God alone, making natural causes mere 'occasions' (Occasionelles). Berkeley reduced causality to the mind. Hume is presented as the most thorough, denying logical necessity and explaining causality as a mental habit from repeated observation. The author, citing Renan, asserts Hume's view is identical to al-Ghazali's. Finally, the passage discusses Ibn Taymiyya, who rejected the Ash'ari 'habit' theory even though he was an enemy of Aristotelian logic. Ibn Taymiyya attributed the critique of causality to Jahm ibn Safwan and disagreed with al-Harawi al-Ansari, who advocated the 'habit' idea before al-Ghazali. This shows an internal Islamic debate: while many Muslim thinkers used the critique of causality to defend miracles, Ibn Taymiyya rejected the 'habit' framework itself.

Key Terms
العلية
العادة
خرق العادة
المعجزات

D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's critique of causality was a theological move to preserve miracles by replacing necessary connection with divine habit.

Comparative Critiques: European Philosophers and Ibn Taymiyya's Objection
Comparative analysis of causality critiques

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the critiques of necessary causality by Malebranche, Berkeley, Hume, and Ibn Taymiyya, and identify the distinct Islamic theological context of Ibn Taymiyya's rejection of the 'ada concept.

Core Idea
Hume's critique mirrors al-Ghazali's, but Ibn Taymiyya rejected even the 'ada framework, deepening the Islamic debate.

The passage shows a remarkable parallel between Islamic and European critiques of causality, but also an internal Islamic disagreement: while al-Ghazali and the Ash'aris embraced the concept of 'ada (habit) to explain regularities, Ibn Taymiyya rejected this concept outright. This lesson explores the views of Malebranche, Berkeley, Hume, and Ibn Taymiyya's unique position.

Guided Path
1
Summarize Malebranche's position
Only God is the true cause; natural causes are merely 'occasional' (cause occasionnelle). He denied any necessary connection in nature.
Malebranche sacrificed logic to preserve divine will and omnipotence.
2
Explain Berkeley's reduction
Berkeley reduced causality to the mind, arguing that we perceive constant conjunction but not necessary connection.
He paved the way for Hume.
3
Analyze Hume's critique
Hume denied logical necessity in causality, attributing it to mental habit from repeated observation. He claimed the idea of cause is nothing but constant conjunction and expectation.
The author, citing Renan, says Hume's view is essentially identical to al-Ghazali's.
4
Contrast with Ibn Taymiyya
Ibn Taymiyya, despite being an enemy of Aristotelian logic, rejected the Ash'ari concept of 'ada and attributed the critique of causality to Jahm ibn Safwan. He denied both necessity and habit as explanations.
This shows a third position within Islam.
5
Identify the underlying issue
For each thinker, the critique serves different ends: for Muslims, defending miracles and divine power; for Europeans, defending divine omnipotence or empiricism; for Ibn Taymiyya, rejecting any compromise that diminishes God's direct creative action.

Al-Ghazali and Hume on Causality: Denying Logical Necessity
Author compares critiques of causality by al-Ghazali and Hume on necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare al-Ghazali's and Hume's critiques of causality and identify the concept of 'āda as an alternative to Aristotelian necessity.

Core Idea
The perceived causal link is just a mental habit of succession.

The passage pairs al-Ghazali and Hume as parallel critics of causal necessity. Renan claimed Hume added nothing new to al-Ghazali's denial of logical necessity. In this lesson, we explore how both thinkers reduced causality to habitual conjunction (āda). This is a foundational critique in Islamic and Western philosophy, challenging the Aristotelian framework of necessary causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Both al-Ghazali and Hume attack the idea of necessary connection between cause and effect. In the passage, find where the author states that al-Ghazali 'denied the necessity between cause and effect.' Note the phrase 'ليست ضرورة منطقية' (not a logical necessity).
2
Extract the alternative explanation
The alternative is 'āda (habit) – the repeated observation of conjunction creates a mental expectation, not a logical deduction. Find the sentence 'تصور العلة ليس في الحقيقة إلا تتابع العادة' (the concept of cause is only habitual succession).
3
Compare the formulations
Note that Hume talks about 'customary succession' and 'mental habit,' while al-Ghazali uses 'āda' and 'iqtirān' (conjunction). The passage states that Hume's critique is similar to that of the Muslims; find the explicit comparison.
Renan's claim is a key point.
4
Reflect on the epistemological shift
Rather than seeking real causal connections, both thinkers ground causality in psychological habit. This shifts the discussion from ontology to epistemology. Consider how this affects proofs of God's existence in kalam.

تريد ذلك الإرادة والحرية الإلهية ، قد ضحى أيضاً هذا الفيلسوف اللاهوتي بالمنطق ، لكي ينقذ الإرادة الإلهية وشمولها . فأنكر « أن توجد إلا علة حقيقية ، لأنه لا يوجد إلا إله حقيقي واحد » وهذه العلة ليست إلا الله ذاته ، وكل العلل الطبيعية ليست عللاً حقيقية ، ولكنها علل مناسبة . Occasionelles . أما بركلي فقد نقد العلية أيضاً ـ بأن أرجعها إلى العقل . وقد مهد هو ومالبرانش الطريق لهيوم . وهيوم أهم هؤلاء الثلاثة في نقده للعلية على العموم . ثم إن نقده للعلية يشبه أيضاً نقد المسلمين لها بل إن رينان يذهب إلى أن هيوم لم يتكلم في هذه الناحية أكثر مما تكلمه الغزالي على الدوام وبإستمرار ـ أي أنكر الضرورة التي بين العلة والمعلول ، وقرر أنها ليست ضرورة منطقية ، ولكنها ضرورة تجريبية فحسب ، يعتمد على مجرد تتابع عادي ، قائم على المشاهدة والملاحظة الحسية . فتصور العلة ليس في الحقيقة إلا تتابع العادة ومنشأ في المخيلة التي لا تستطيع في هذا الميدان أن تعمل بحرية كما تستطيع هذا العمل في ميدان الخرافات . فإذا ما رأينا دائماً فكرة تتابع أخرى ، فإننا نصطلح على اعتبار الأولى علة ، وننتظر أن نرى الأخرى تتبعها . وفي هذه الحالة نسميها معلولاً وإذا تكرر تلازم الحدوث وتكررت ملاحظتنا لهذا التلازم . تكونت لنا هذه العادة العقلية التي هي فقط أساس تصورنا لبث العلية . وكأن هيوم قد قرأ الغزالي ، ونقل عنه فكرته نقلاً تاماً . وقد حاول ابن تيمية أن ينسب نقد العلية عند الأشاعرة وعند الهروي الأنصاري الشيخ السلفي القديم إلى جهم بن صفوان . ومن العجب أن نرى ابن تيمية هذا العدو الممتاز للمنطق الارسططاليسي ـ كما سنرى فيما بعد ، ينكر فكرة العادة التي نادى بها الأشاعرة ، كما نادى بها شيخه وهو ـ الهروي الانصاري . لقد نادى الهروي الأنصاري قبل الغزالي بفكرة العادة فقال في نص من أجمل النصوص « ليس في الوجود شيء يكون سبباً لشيء أصلاً ولا شيء جعل لشيء ، ولا يكون شيء جعل لأصل شيء ، ولا يكون شيء بشيء ، فالشبع لا يكون بالأكل ولا العلم الحاصل في القلب بالدليل ولا ما يحصل للمتوكل من الرزق له سبب أصلاً لا في نفسه ولا في نفس الأمر ، بل محض الإرادة الواحدة يصدر عنها كل حادث ، ويصدر مع الآخر مقترناً به اقتراناً عادياً لا أن أحدهما معلق بالآخر أو سبب له أو حكمة له ، ولكن لأجل ما جرت به العادة من اقتران أحدهما بالآخر ، بجعل أحدهما أمارة وعلماً ودليلاً بمعنى أنه وجد أحد المقترنين عادة كان الآخر موجوداً معه ، وليس العلم الحاصل في القلب حاصلاً بهذا الدليل ، بل هذا أيضاً من جملة الاقترانات العادة » نستخلص من هذا أن المدرسة السلفية الحقيقية أنكرت أيضاً العلية الأرسططاليسية وتوصلت أيضاً إلى فكرة الإقتران والعادة . وأنكر ابن تيمية هذا كما رأينا وكان موقفه هذا غير متناسق مع هجومه العنيف على المنطق الأرسططاليسي .
[The divine will and freedom wanted that; this philosophical theologian also sacrificed logic in order to save the divine will and its comprehensiveness. He denied that there is any true cause except one, because there is only one true God. And this cause is none other than God Himself, and all natural causes are not true causes, but occasional causes. As for Berkeley, he also criticized causality by reducing it to the mind. He and Malebranche paved the way for Hume. And Hume is the most important of these three in his critique of causality in general. Then his critique of causality also resembles the critique of the Muslims; indeed, Renan goes so far as to say that Hume did not say more in this regard than what al-Ghazali said constantly and continuously — that is, he denied the necessity between cause and effect, and determined that it is not a logical necessity, but merely an empirical necessity, based on mere customary succession, grounded in sensory observation and perception. So the concept of cause is in reality only habitual succession and originates in the imagination, which cannot in this field act freely as it can in the field of superstitions. When we always see one idea following another, we agree to consider the first a cause, and we expect to see the other follow it. In this case we call it an effect; and when the conjunction of occurrence is repeated and our observation of this conjunction is repeated, this mental habit is formed, which is only the basis of our concept of causation. It is as if Hume had read al-Ghazali and fully transmitted his idea. And Ibn Taymiyya tried to attribute the critique of causality of the Ash'aris and of al-Harawi al-Ansari, the ancient Salafi shaykh, to Jahm ibn Safwan. And it is surprising to see Ibn Taymiyya, this excellent enemy of Aristotelian logic — as we will see later — denying the idea of habit which the Ash'aris advocated, as advocated by his shaykh, al-Harawi al-Ansari. Indeed, al-Harawi al-Ansari before al-Ghazali advocated the idea of habit and said in a most beautiful text: 'There is nothing in existence that is a cause for anything at all, nor anything made for anything, nor is anything made for an original thing, nor is anything by anything. So satiety does not occur from eating, nor the knowledge acquired in the heart from the proof, nor what the one who trusts obtains of provision has any cause at all, neither in itself nor in the matter itself; rather, the pure single will brings about every event, and it brings about the other concomitant with it by habitual conjunction, not that one is dependent on the other or a cause for it or a wisdom for it, but because of the habit that has occurred of one being conjoined with the other, by making one a sign, a mark, and an indication, meaning that when one of the two conjoined things exists habitually, the other exists with it. And the knowledge acquired in the heart is not acquired through this proof, but this too is among the habitual conjunctions.' We conclude from this that the true Salafi school also denied Aristotelian causality and also arrived at the idea of conjunction and habit. And Ibn Taymiyya denied this as we saw, and this position of his was inconsistent with his violent attack on Aristotelian logic.]
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition on the critique of Aristotelian causality. The author begins by noting that Malebranche sacrificed logic to preserve divine will, denying real causality except in God, and reducing natural causes to occasional causes. He then states that Berkeley reduced causality to the mind, and that both paved the way for Hume. Hume's critique is highlighted as similar to that of Muslim thinkers, specifically al-Ghazali, with Renan claiming Hume said no more than al-Ghazali on the denial of necessary connection between cause and effect. Hume argued that causality is not a logical necessity but only a habit of succession based on repeated observation. The author then turns to Ibn Taymiyya's attempt to attribute the critique of causality among the Ash'aris and al-Harawi al-Ansari to Jahm ibn Safwan. Interestingly, Ibn Taymiyya, known for his vehement opposition to Aristotelian logic, denied the 'habit' idea that the Ash'aris and his own shaykh al-Harawi had advocated. The passage concludes with a lengthy quote from al-Harawi explicitly denying any real causal connection in existence, asserting that all events occur by the direct will of God, with only habitual conjunction between them. The author notes the inconsistency in Ibn Taymiyya's position: he attacked Aristotelian logic yet rejected the very idea of habit that underlies the critique of causality. This shows the complexity of Islamic intellectual history regarding causality and logic.

Key Terms
علة
عادة
اقتران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Ghazali deny about the relationship between cause and effect?
A) That there is any relationship
B) That it is a logical necessity
C) That it is an empirical necessity
D) That God is the only cause

Key Takeaway
The critique of Aristotelian causality by both al-Ghazali and Hume reduces necessity to a mental habit, challenging the foundation of logical reasoning about cause and effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the passage's description, reconstruct al-Ghazali's argument against necessary causality in one or two syllogistic steps. (Hint: Premise: The only necessary cause is God. Observation: Fire and burning are always conjoined. Conclusion: The conjunction is habitual, not necessary.)
?
Compare the statement of al-Harawi: 'ليس في الوجود شيء يكون سبباً لشيء' (Nothing in existence is a cause for anything) with Hume's statement from the passage. What key difference or similarity do you notice regarding the role of God?

intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Inconsistent Stance on Causality and Logic
Author discusses Ibn Taymiyya's denial of the habit idea despite his attack on Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's position on Ash'ari occasionalism and evaluate its inconsistency with his critique of Aristotelian logic.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacked Aristotelian logic but rejected the Ash'ari critique of causality that he shared.

The passage highlights a striking inconsistency in Ibn Taymiyya: while he is a fierce opponent of Aristotelian logic, he denies the very idea of habitual conjunction (āda) that the Ash'aris and his own teacher al-Harawi used to refute Aristotelian causality. This lesson examines this tension and what it reveals about the complexity of Islamic intellectual debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's overall stance on Aristotelian logic
The passage calls him 'العدو الممتاز للمنطق الأرسططاليسي' (the excellent enemy of Aristotelian logic). This sets up his identity.
2
Find his specific denial of 'āda
The passage says 'أنكر ابن تيمية هذا' (Ibn Taymiyya denied this) – referring to the habit idea. Locate this phrase.
3
Recognize that al-Harawi, his shaykh, promoted 'āda
The passage quotes al-Harawi's strong occasionalism. This shows that Ibn Taymiyya's denial contradicts his own teacher's position.
4
Assess the inconsistency
Attacking Aristotelian logic while rejecting a key argument against that logic seems self-contradictory. The passage suggests his position was not coherent. Consider why a Salafi thinker might reject 'āda (maybe because it diminishes the efficacy of secondary causes that are affirmed in some Salafi views).

The Salafi Occasionalist View of Causality
A quote denying causality; author's critique of Ibn Taymiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key tenets of the occasionalist theory of causality as presented in the quoted text and distinguish it from Aristotelian causality.

Core Idea
For the true Salafi school, satiety does not come from eating; both are merely conjoined by divine custom.

The passage opens with a striking text that denies any real causal connection between events. Everything is directly from God's will, and what we perceive as causes are merely habitual conjunctions. This occasionalist view was adopted by the Ash'ari and — according to the author — by the true Salafi school. Let's dissect the argument to understand its theological and logical import.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central denial
Notice that the quote denies causality in all realms: eating does not cause satiety, evidence does not cause knowledge, and provision does not come from effort. It asserts that no thing is a cause for any other thing.
2
Extract the positive claim
The quote explains that events are conjoined by divine custom (al-iqtirān al-‘ādī). Satiety and eating occur together habitually, but neither is the cause of the other.
3
Understand the theological motive
This view preserves divine omnipotence by avoiding attributing any efficacy to secondary causes. Everything originates from the single divine will.
This is a form of occasionalism, also held by the Ash'ari school.
4
Recognize the school of thought
The author calls this the 'true Salafi school' and links it to the Ash'ari occasionalist tradition. This is distinct from the later Salafi movement.
5
Prepare for the contrast
The author will later note that Ibn Taymiyya rejected this occasionalist view, creating an inconsistency with his critique of Aristotelian logic.
This sets up the critical analysis in the following lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نص من أجمل النصوص « ليس في الوجود شيء يكون سبباً لشيء أصلاً ولا شيء جعل لشيء ، ولا يكون شيء جعل لأصل شيء ، ولا يكون شيء بشيء ، فالشبع لا يكون بالأكل ولا العلم الحاصل في القلب بالدليل ولا ما يحصل للمتوكل من الرزق له سبب أصلاً لا في نفسه ولا في نفس الأمر ، بل محض الإرادة الواحدة يصدر عنها كل حادث ، ويصدر مع الآخر مقترناً به اقتراناً عادياً لا أن أحدهما معلق بالآخر أو سبب له أو حكمة له ، ولكن لأجل ما جرت به العادة من اقتران أحدهما بالآخر ، بجعل أحدهما أمارة وعلماً ودليلاً بمعنى أنه وجد أحد المقترنين عادة كان الآخر موجوداً معه ، وليس العلم الحاصل في القلب حاصلاً بهذا الدليل ، بل هذا أيضاً من جملة الاقترانات العادة » نستخلص من هذا أن المدرسة السلفية الحقيقية أنكرت أيضاً العلية الأرسططاليسية وتوصلت أيضاً إلى فكرة الإقتران والعادة . وأنكر ابن تيمية هذا كما رأينا(١) وكان موقفه هذا غير متناسق مع هجومه العنيف على المنطق الأرسططاليسي .
One of the most beautiful texts: 'There is nothing in existence that is a cause for anything at all, nor is anything made for something, nor is anything made for the origin of something, nor is anything by something. So satiety does not occur by eating, nor does the knowledge obtained in the heart by the evidence, nor does what the one who trusts obtains of provision have any cause at all, neither in itself nor in the matter itself. Rather, it is mere single will from which every event originates, and it originates with the other, conjoined with it by habitual conjunction, not that one of them is dependent on the other, or a cause for it, or a wisdom for it. But because of what custom has established of the conjunction of one with the other, by making one a sign, a mark, and an indication, meaning that when one of the two conjuncts is found customarily, the other exists with it. And the knowledge obtained in the heart is not obtained by this evidence; rather, this too is among the customary conjunctions.' We conclude from this that the true Salafi school also denied Aristotelian causality and also arrived at the idea of conjunction and habit. And Ibn Taymiyya denied this, as we have seen, and his position was inconsistent with his violent attack on Aristotelian logic.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage begins with a lengthy quotation from an unnamed source, likely an Ash'ari occasionalist text, which vigorously denies any real causality in the world. The quote argues that satiety does not come from eating, knowledge does not come from evidence, and provision does not come from effort—all events are directly created by the single divine will and merely accompany each other by habitual conjunction. The author then comments that this represents the true Salafi school, which rejected Aristotelian causality in favor of the theory of conjunction and habit. However, the author points out an inconsistency: Ibn Taymiyya, who is known for his fierce attack on Aristotelian logic, actually denied this occasionalist view (as seen in a reference to his Minhaj al-Sunna). According to the author, Ibn Taymiyya's acceptance of causality contradicts his critique of the very logic that is built upon causality, revealing a lack of coherence in his position.

Ibn Taymiyya's Inconsistent Position on Causality
Author critiques Ibn Taymiyya's inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the logical inconsistency in Ibn Taymiyya's position on causality as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's attack on Aristotelian logic clashes with his denial of occasionalist causality.

The author of our text points out a striking inconsistency in Ibn Taymiyya's thought. On one hand, Ibn Taymiyya fiercely attacked Aristotelian logic; on the other, he rejected the occasionalist view of causation that many of his Salafi predecessors held. This lesson unpacks that tension and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Recall the occasionalist position
From the previous lesson, recall that the occasionalist view denies real causality; events are conjoined by habit, not causation. This is the position the author attributes to the true Salafi school.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's denial
The author states that Ibn Taymiyya denied this occasionalist view, referencing his work Minhaj al-Sunna. This means Ibn Taymiyya accepted that there is real causal connection between events.
3
Identify the inconsistency
Aristotelian logic is built on the concept of causality (e.g., syllogisms rely on causal connections between terms). By rejecting occasionalism, Ibn Taymiyya implicitly accepts causality, undermining his own attack on Aristotelian logic.
4
Consider the broader implications
This inconsistency suggests that Ibn Taymiyya's critique of logic may have been selective or motivated by other concerns. For a coherent theological method, one must be consistent across foundational issues.
5
Evaluate the critique's strength
Does the author's point hold? If Aristotelian logic indeed presupposes causality, then Ibn Taymiyya's position seems contradictory. However, one could argue that logic does not necessarily require Aristotelian causality; it may only require formal relations.
This step encourages critical thinking about both sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نص من أجمل النصوص « ليس في الوجود شيء يكون سبباً لشيء أصلاً ولا شيء جعل لشيء ، ولا يكون شيء جعل لأصل شيء ، ولا يكون شيء بشيء ، فالشبع لا يكون بالأكل ولا العلم الحاصل في القلب بالدليل ولا ما يحصل للمتوكل من الرزق له سبب أصلاً لا في نفسه ولا في نفس الأمر ، بل محض الإرادة الواحدة يصدر عنها كل حادث ، ويصدر مع الآخر مقترناً به اقتراناً عادياً لا أن أحدهما معلق بالآخر أو سبب له أو حكمة له ، ولكن لأجل ما جرت به العادة من اقتران أحدهما بالآخر ، بجعل أحدهما أمارة وعلماً ودليلاً بمعنى أنه وجد أحد المقترنين عادة كان الآخر موجوداً معه ، وليس العلم الحاصل في القلب حاصلاً بهذا الدليل ، بل هذا أيضاً من جملة الاقترانات العادة » نستخلص من هذا أن المدرسة السلفية الحقيقية أنكرت أيضاً العلية الأرسططاليسية وتوصلت أيضاً إلى فكرة الإقتران والعادة . وأنكر ابن تيمية هذا كما رأينا(١) وكان موقفه هذا غير متناسق مع هجومه العنيف على المنطق الأرسططاليسي .
One of the most beautiful texts: 'There is nothing in existence that is a cause for anything at all, nor is anything made for something, nor is anything made for the origin of something, nor is anything by something. So satiety does not occur by eating, nor does the knowledge obtained in the heart by the evidence, nor does what the one who trusts obtains of provision have any cause at all, neither in itself nor in the matter itself. Rather, it is mere single will from which every event originates, and it originates with the other, conjoined with it by habitual conjunction, not that one of them is dependent on the other, or a cause for it, or a wisdom for it. But because of what custom has established of the conjunction of one with the other, by making one a sign, a mark, and an indication, meaning that when one of the two conjuncts is found customarily, the other exists with it. And the knowledge obtained in the heart is not obtained by this evidence; rather, this too is among the customary conjunctions.' We conclude from this that the true Salafi school also denied Aristotelian causality and also arrived at the idea of conjunction and habit. And Ibn Taymiyya denied this, as we have seen, and his position was inconsistent with his violent attack on Aristotelian logic.

Ibn al-Salah's Fatwa: The Jurists' Rejection of Aristotelian Logic
Quotes Ibn al-Salah's fatwa prohibiting study and teaching of Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn al-Salah's argument against Aristotelian logic and identify its implications for the study of usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Salah deemed Aristotelian logic an innovation and forbade its mixing with Islamic sciences.

Ibn al-Salah's fatwa represents a decisive moment in the Islamic intellectual tradition's confrontation with Greek logic. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and evaluates its impact on usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of the fatwa
Read the question addressed to Ibn al-Salah: it asks about studying and teaching logic/philosophy, using logical terminology in legal rulings, and the ruler's duty towards logicians. Understand the specific concerns raised.
The question sets the scope of the fatwa.
2
Extract the main prohibitions
Ibn al-Salah forbids learning and teaching logic, using its terminology in legal discussions, and mixing it with Islamic sciences. He calls these 'abominable innovations'.
3
Analyze his dismissal of hadd and burhan
Ibn al-Salah calls the logicians' claims about definition and proof 'bubbles' that Allah has rendered unnecessary. Identify why he considers them superfluous for Islamic scholars.
Hadd and burhan are central Aristotelian concepts.
4
Assess the implications for usul al-fiqh
The author notes that the fatwa implies prohibition of studying usul books that mix logic with fiqh, such as al-Burhan and al-Mustasfa. Understand how the fatwa targets specific works.
5
Evaluate the practical consequences
Ibn al-Salah demands that the ruler expel teachers of Aristotelian logic from schools and force them to repent under threat of execution. Consider the severity of the stance.
This shows the fatwa is not merely advisory but carries coercive implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعطينا كل هذا فكرة واضحة عن موقف الفقهاء من المنطق الأرسططاليسي ومعارضتهم له ، بحيث أصبح شائعاً القول « من تمنطق تزندق » . وفي وسط هذه البيئة المعارضة أطلق ابن الصلاح فتواه المشهورة ضد المنطق . فقد وجه إلى ابن الصلاح سؤال عن « الاشتغال بالمنطق والفلسفة تعلماً وتعليماً . هل أباحه واستباحه الصحابة والتابعون والأئمة المجتهدون والسلف الصالحون ؟ وهل يجوز استخدام الاصطلاحات المنطقية أم لا ؟ وما الواجب على من تلبس بتعليمه وتعلمه متظاهراً به . وما الذي يجب على سلطان الوقت في أمره ؟ وإذا وجد في بعض البلاد شخص من أهل الفلسفة معروف بتعليمها وإقرائها والتصنيف فيها ـ فهل يجب على سلطان البلد عزله وكفاية الناس شره ؟ »(۱) . أجاب ابن الصلاح على هذا بأن « المنطق مدخل الفلسفة ومدخل الشر . وليس الاشتغال بتعليمه وتعلمه مما أباحه الشارع ولا استباحه أحد من الصحابة والتابعين والأئمة المجتهدين والسلف الصالحين وسائر من يقتدى به »(۲) . ثم يجيب ابن الصلاح عن النقطة الثانية من السؤال وهي استخدام الاصطلاحات المنطقية في مباحث الأحكام الشرعية فيقول إنها « من المنكرات المستبشعة والرقاعات المستحدثة وليس بالأحكام الشرعية افتقار إلى المنطق أصلاً . وما يزعمه المنطقي بالمنطق من أمر الحد والبرهان ففقاقع قد أغنى عنها الله كل صحيح الذهن لا سيما من خدم نظريات العلوم الشرعية . ولقد تمت الشريعة وعلومها ، وخاض في بحر الحقائق والدقائق علماؤها ، حيث لا منطق ولا فلسفة ولا فلاسفة . ومن زعم أنه يشتغل مع نفسه بالمنطق والفلسفة لفائدة يزعمها فقد خدعه الشيطان »(۱) وهذا النص الذي أوردته من كلام ابن الصلاح ذو أهمية كبيرة للغاية . لأنه إذا كان مزج العلوم الشرعية بالمنطق من البدع والمنكرات ، نشأ عن هذا تحريم النظر في كتب أصول الفقه ، التي مزج فيها الأصول بالمنطق أمثال البرهان والمستصفى وغيرهما من الكتب الأصولية الهامة . إن فتوى أخرى لابن الصلاح تثبت هذا تمام الإثبات . فقد سئل عن كتاب من كتب الأصول ليس فيه شيء من علم الكلام ولا من المنطق ولا ما يتعلق بغير أصول الفقه - هل يحرم الاشتغال به أو يكره ؟ وفي الواقع إن المقصود بهذا السؤال هو الجانب السلبي من المسألة . أي عدم إباحة دراسة كتب الأصول الممتزجة بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي . أجاب ابن الصلاح بأن كتب الأصول إذا خلت من منطق أو فلسفة فمن المباح دراستها(۲) . ويؤخذ منه أنه يجب تحريم كتب الأصول الممتزجة بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي . ثم يكمل ابن الصلاح فتواه ، بأن على ولي الأمر أن يخرج معلمي المنطق الأرسططاليسي من المدارس ، وأن يعرضهم على السيف حتى يستتيبوا .
All this gives us a clear idea of the position of the jurists towards Aristotelian logic and their opposition to it, such that the saying 'Whoever becomes a logician becomes a heretic' became widespread. In the midst of this opposing environment, Ibn al-Salah issued his famous fatwa against logic. A question was directed to Ibn al-Salah about 'occupying oneself with logic and philosophy, learning and teaching. Did the Companions, the Followers, the independent jurists, and the righteous predecessors permit it? Is it permissible to use logical terminology or not? What is the duty of one who is engaged in teaching and learning it openly? What is the duty of the ruler of the time in this matter? And if there is found in some country a person from the philosophers known for teaching it, having it read, and writing about it – must the ruler of the country dismiss him and spare the people his evil?' (1). Ibn al-Salah answered that 'logic is the entrance to philosophy and the entrance to evil. Occupying oneself with its teaching and learning is not something the Lawgiver has permitted, nor did any of the Companions, Followers, independent jurists, righteous predecessors, and all who are emulated, permit it' (2). Then Ibn al-Salah answered the second part of the question, which is the use of logical terminology in discussions of legal rulings, saying it is 'among the abominable wrong actions and newly invented innovations. Legal rulings have no need for logic at all. What the logician claims about definition and proof (hadd and burhan) are bubbles that Allah has made unnecessary for anyone of sound mind, especially those who serve the theories of the religious sciences. Indeed, the Sharia and its sciences have been completed, and its scholars have waded into the sea of truths and subtleties where there is no logic, no philosophy, and no philosophers. Whoever claims that he occupies himself with logic and philosophy for some supposed benefit, Satan has deceived him' (1). And this text that I have quoted from Ibn al-Salah's speech is of great importance. Because if mixing the religious sciences with logic is among innovations and wrong actions, this would entail the prohibition of looking at books of usul al-fiqh that mix usul with logic, such as al-Burhan, al-Mustasfa, and other important usul books. Another fatwa from Ibn al-Salah proves this completely. He was asked about a book of usul that contains nothing of kalam, logic, or anything not pertaining to usul al-fiqh – is studying it prohibited or disliked? In reality, the intent of this question is the negative aspect of the issue, i.e., the non-permissibility of studying usul books mixed with Aristotelian logic. Ibn al-Salah answered that if usul books are free of logic or philosophy, it is permissible to study them (2). It is understood from this that books of usul mixed with Aristotelian logic must be prohibited. Then Ibn al-Salah completes his fatwa by stating that the ruler must remove teachers of Aristotelian logic from schools and expose them to the sword until they repent.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the historical context of opposition to Aristotelian logic in the Islamic world, specifically through the fatwa of Ibn al-Salah. The passage begins by noting the widespread opposition among jurists, epitomized by the saying 'Whoever becomes a logician becomes a heretic.' The core of the passage is a question addressed to Ibn al-Salah, asking about the permissibility of studying and teaching logic and philosophy, the use of logical terminology in Islamic legal rulings, and the duty of the ruler regarding those who practice philosophy. Ibn al-Salah's response is unequivocal: logic is the gateway to evil, the study of it was never permitted by the early generations of Muslims, and the use of logical terminology in legal matters is a detestable innovation. He dismisses the logicians' claims that their science provides valuable tools for definition (hadd) and proof (burhan), calling them 'bubbles' that Allah has rendered unnecessary for anyone with a sound mind, especially scholars of religious sciences. The author then comments that this fatwa implies the prohibition of studying usul al-fiqh works that incorporate logic, such as al-Burhan and al-Mustasfa. Ibn al-Salah reinforces this by stating that books of usul free from logic are permissible, while those mixed with it are prohibited. Finally, Ibn al-Salah demands that the ruler expel teachers of Aristotelian logic from schools and force them to repent under threat of execution. The commentary follows the author's own order: first the background, then the fatwa itself, then the author's analysis of its implications. All examples mentioned (like the usul books) are from Islamic sciences.

المنطق الأرسططاليسي
الحد والبرهان
المنكرات المستبشعة

The Far-Reaching Impact of Ibn al-Salah's Fatwa on Logic
Author describes the profound impact of Ibn al-Salah's fatwa and the resulting division of scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the historical impact of Ibn al-Salah's fatwa on later logic texts and identify the new debate it generated.

Core Idea
A single fatwa reshaped the structure of logic textbooks for centuries.

We have just examined the elements of Ibn al-Salah's famous fatwa against logic. Now the author turns to its aftermath. He tells us that this fatwa had such a profound effect that a new question appears in the introductions of later logic books—a direct result of the controversy. This shows how a legal ruling can transform the very curriculum of an Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Trace the causal chain from fatwa to textbook change
Identify the fatwa's claim (Ibn al-Salah declared logic prohibited). Then note the author's statement: it had an 'immense impact' leading to a new discussion in later logic books. What is that new discussion?
The new discussion is explicitly stated: 'Is it permissible to occupy oneself with logic or not?'
2
Recognize the transformation of introductory material
Earlier logic books did not begin with a permissibility question. This fatwa made it necessary for later scholars to address the issue before proceeding. Observe how the fatwa changed even the structure of textbooks.
3
Identify the division among scholars
The author begins to note that those who prohibit logic are divided into two groups. Although the passage cuts off, anticipate a classification based on degrees of prohibition or reasons.
This division shows the fatwa did not create a simple binary, but a spectrum of positions.
4
Reflect on the significance of including a permissibility question in a logic text
Why would a logic book start by asking about its own permissibility? This indicates that the study of logic itself was contested. Understanding this context helps explain the defensive tone in many later logic works.
Compare with modern Islamic studies where certain subjects may require justification.

Ibn al-Salah's Fatwa Against Aristotelian Logic
Ibn al-Salah's fatwa against Aristotelian logic and its aftermath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two main arguments for prohibiting Aristotelian logic according to Ibn al-Salah's fatwa and evaluate their internal consistency.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Salah's prohibition of logic is not about its formal techniques but about its harmful metaphysical content.

Ibn al-Salah's fatwa against Aristotelian logic had a profound impact on Islamic scholarship, leading later logicians to begin their works with a defense of logic's permissibility. In this lesson, we will dissect the fatwa's reasoning, distinguishing the two major arguments used to prohibit logic, and assess their logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Ibn al-Salah asserts that mixing religious sciences with logic is an innovation (bid'a) and that sound minds have no need for logic.
The claim is about the act of studying logic, not its content per se.
2
Extract the two supporting arguments
The first group prohibits logic because it is a science of Jews and Christians. The second group rejects that reasoning and instead prohibits logic because it is mixed with false metaphysics that can corrupt beliefs.
The second group refines the fatwa by giving a more principled reason.
3
Compare the two arguments
The first argument is criticized as too broad (would also prohibit medicine and eating). The second argument is considered stronger because it targets the specific harmful content.
The second group uses the principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means) to justify absolute prohibition.
4
Evaluate the second argument's use of sadd al-dhara'i'
Consider whether logic inevitably leads to metaphysical corruption, or whether it can be separated from false beliefs. This determines the validity of the absolute prohibition.
Later scholars often distinguish between logic as a tool and its philosophical presuppositions.
5
Assess internal validity
Both arguments rely on empirical claims (e.g., that logic necessarily corrupts). Check if these claims are supported by evidence or are overly cautious.
The internal validity of the fatwa's reasoning depends on whether the harms are unavoidable.

وما يزعمه المنطقي بالمنطق من أمر الحد والبرهان ففقاقع قد أغنى عنها الله كل صحيح الذهن لا سيما من خدم نظريات العلوم الشرعية . ولقد تمت الشريعة وعلومها ، وخاض في بحر الحقائق والدقائق علماؤها ، حيث لا منطق ولا فلسفة ولا فلاسفة . ومن زعم أنه يشتغل مع نفسه بالمنطق والفلسفة لفائدة يزعمها فقد خدعه الشيطان(۱) . وهذا النص الذي أوردته من كلام ابن الصلاح ذو أهمية كبيرة للغاية . لأنه إذا كان مزج العلوم الشرعية بالمنطق من البدع والمنكرات ، نشأ عن هذا تحريم النظر في كتب أصول الفقه ، التي مزج فيها الأصول بالمنطق أمثال البرهان والمستصفى وغيرهما من الكتب الأصولية الهامة . إن فتوى أخرى لابن الصلاح تثبت هذا تمام الإثبات . فقد سئل عن كتاب من كتب الأصول ليس فيه شيء من علم الكلام ولا من المنطق ولا ما يتعلق بغير أصول الفقه - هل يحرم الاشتغال به أو يكره ؟ وفي الواقع إن المقصود بهذا السؤال هو الجانب السلبي من المسألة . أي عدم إباحة دراسة كتب الأصول الممتزجة بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي . أجاب ابن الصلاح بأن كتب الأصول إذا خلت من منطق أو فلسفة فمن المباح دراستها(۲) . ويؤخذ منه أنه يجب تحريم كتب الأصول الممتزجة بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي . ثم يكمل ابن الصلاح فتواه ، بأن على ولي الأمر أن يخرج معلمي المنطق الأرسططاليسي من المدارس ، وأن يعرضهم على السيف حتى يستتيبوا . تلك هي عناصر فتوى ابن الصلاح كان لها من الأثر البالغ في العالم الإسلامي ما لا يتصور بحيث نرى بحثاً جديداً في أول الكتب المنطقية المتأخرة وهو : هل يجوز الاشتغال بالمنطق أو لا يجوز(۳) . وينقسم القائلون بتحريمه إلى قسمين : القسم الأول : يرى أن المنطق الأرسططاليسي من علوم اليهود والنصارى ولا يجوز الاشتغال بعلوم هؤلاء . القسم الثاني : يرى أن القول بتحريم المنطق لاشتغال اليهود والنصارى به قول خاطىء ، لأن معنى هذا تحريم الطب وتحريم الأكل والشرب ، لأن اليهود والنصارى يشتغلون بهما . وإنما العلة في تحريمه هو أنه إذا كان ممزوجاً بإلهيات الفلاسفة المخالفة لعقائد المسلمين ، يخشى على المشتغل به من تمكين تلك العقائد الفلسفية فيه فيؤدي به إلى الإبتداع ـ كما هو عند المعتزلة أو الكفر ـ كما هو عند الفلاسفة(١) . وإذا قيل لهؤلاء : إن من يؤمن عنده الفتنة والنزاع « ممارس السنة والكتاب » لا يحرم عليه تعلم المنطق . قالوا بأنه يجب تحريم المنطق الأرسططاليسي أصلاً أمناً للفتنة وسداً للذرائع(٢) .
And what the logician claims regarding logic about definition and demonstration are bubbles; Allah has made every sound mind independent of them, especially those who serve the theories of the religious sciences. The Sharia and its sciences have been perfected, and its scholars have delved into the sea of truths and subtleties where there is no logic, no philosophy, and no philosophers. Whoever claims that he occupies himself with logic and philosophy for a benefit he alleges has been deceived by Satan (1). This text I have quoted from Ibn al-Salah's speech is of great importance. Because if mixing religious sciences with logic is among innovations and disapproved acts, then this results in prohibiting the study of books of usul al-fiqh that mix usul with logic, such as al-Burhan, al-Mustasfa, and other important usul books. Another fatwa of Ibn al-Salah confirms this completely. He was asked about a book of usul that contains nothing of kalam or logic or anything related to other than usul al-fiqh: is it prohibited or disliked to occupy oneself with it? In fact, the intent of this question is the negative aspect of the issue, i.e., the non-permissibility of studying books of usul mixed with Aristotelian logic. Ibn al-Salah answered that if books of usul are free from logic or philosophy, then studying them is permissible (2). From this it is understood that books of usul mixed with Aristotelian logic must be prohibited. Then Ibn al-Salah completes his fatwa, stating that the ruler must expel teachers of Aristotelian logic from schools and expose them to the sword until they repent. These are the elements of Ibn al-Salah's fatwa; it had such a profound effect on the Islamic world that we see a new discussion at the beginning of later logic books: is it permissible to occupy oneself with logic or not (3)? And those who say it is prohibited are divided into two groups: First group: they hold that Aristotelian logic is among the sciences of the Jews and Christians, and it is not permissible to occupy oneself with their sciences. Second group: they hold that the statement prohibiting logic because Jews and Christians occupy themselves with it is a mistaken statement, because this would imply prohibition of medicine and prohibition of eating and drinking, since Jews and Christians engage in them. The real reason for its prohibition is that if it is mixed with the metaphysics of the philosophers that contradict Muslim beliefs, the one occupied with it may become influenced by those philosophical beliefs, leading to innovation—as with the Mu'tazila—or unbelief—as with the philosophers (1). And if it is said to them: 'The one who is safe from temptation and conflict, the practitioner of the Sunna and the Book, is not prohibited from learning logic,' they say that Aristotelian logic must be prohibited absolutely, as a security against temptation and closing the means (2).
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn al-Salah's fatwa against Aristotelian logic and its impact on Islamic scholarship. The author begins by quoting Ibn al-Salah's harsh condemnation: the logician's claims about definition and demonstration are like bubbles, and Allah has made sound minds independent of them. Ibn al-Salah praises the early scholars who mastered the religious sciences without any need for logic or philosophy. He then derives from this that mixing religious sciences with logic is an innovation, which leads to prohibiting the study of usul al-fiqh books that have logic mixed in, such as al-Burhan and al-Mustasfa. The author reports another fatwa: when asked about a usul book free from kalam and logic, Ibn al-Salah permitted studying it, implying that mixed books are prohibited. He even calls for the expulsion of logic teachers under threat of death. The passage then notes the lasting effect of this fatwa: later logic books begin with the question of whether studying logic is permissible. The fatwa gave rise to two groups among those who prohibit logic: the first group forbids it because it is a science of Jews and Christians, while the second group refines this reasoning, arguing that prohibition is not because of its practitioners but because logic, when mixed with false metaphysics, leads to innovation (bid‘a) or unbelief (kufr). They insist on absolute prohibition as a precautionary measure to block means (sadd al-dhara’i‘) that could lead to fitna.

Key Terms
المنطق الأرسططاليسي
أصول الفقه
فتوى
بدعة
ذريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second group in Ibn al-Salah's fatwa, what is the real reason for prohibiting Aristotelian logic?
A) It is the science of Jews and Christians.
B) It leads to innovation or unbelief when mixed with false metaphysics.
C) It is useless and has no benefit.
D) It contradicts the Quran and Sunna directly.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Salah's prohibition of logic rested on two distinct arguments: one based on the practitioners' identity, the other on the risk of metaphysical corruption; the latter became the more influential justification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Taymiyya's Methodological Shift: From Fatwa to Rational Critique
Ibn Taymiyya's methodological shift from fatwa to rational critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's methodological critique from earlier fatwa-based prohibitions of logic, and identify both his destructive and constructive contributions.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacked logic not just as un-Islamic, but as irrational, and built a rival Islamic logic.

This passage describes a pivotal turn in Islamic critiques of Aristotelian logic. While earlier scholars like Ibn al-Salah issued fatwas forbidding its study, Ibn Taymiyya raised the critique to a methodological level, arguing that logic contradicts both revelation and clear reason. Importantly, he did not stop at demolition: he attempted to construct an alternative Islamic logic grounded in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier approach
Find the description of Ibn al-Salah's influence: the passage says his effect was profound, and the earlier critique took the form of fatwas prohibiting logic. Note that this is a legal/religious prohibition, not a rational one.
2
Locate the shift in Ibn Taymiyya's method
The passage states the attack 'began to take the form of methodological criticism based on logical foundations.' Identify the two grounds on which Ibn Taymiyya rejects logic: it contradicts 'sahih al-manqul' (sound transmitted knowledge) and 'sarih al-ma‘qul' (clear rational knowledge). This dual basis is the key shift.
Contrast with Ibn al-Salah who only used the first ground.
3
Separate destructive and constructive aspects
The passage explicitly says Ibn Taymiyya's work was not only destructive but also had a 'constructive side' (janib insha’i). Find the sentence: 'he tried to set alongside his critique ... another Islamic logic.' This shows his project was not purely negative.
4
Recognize external influences
The passage compares Ibn Taymiyya to 'empirical skeptics' (shukak tajribiyyin) who influenced the destructive elements of his critique. Note that this suggests a philosophical lineage, though the passage does not elaborate.
This comparison is a scholarly claim; not all scholars accept it.
5
Consider the incomplete passage
The Arabic text ends abruptly with 'فيكاد يكون' (it almost is...). Reflect on what the missing continuation might have discussed: likely more details about the constructive aspect or the remaining destructive elements.
This is a caution: our analysis is based on a truncated text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إننا نرى من كل هذا أن أثر ابن الصلاح كان عميقا في علماء المسلمين . ولكن ما لبث نقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي أن توجه وجهة أخرى على يد مفكر السلف المتأخر تقي الدين بن تيمية ( ٦٢١هـ ) فلم يعد الهجوم يتمثل على المنطق الأرسططاليسي في صورة فتاوى تقرر تحريمه وعدم الاشتغال به ، بل بدأ الهجوم يتخذ شكل النقد المنهجي ويقوم على أسس منطقية ، أو بمعنى آخر لم يكتف ابن تيمية بالقول بأن المنطق يخالف صحيح المنقول ، بل اعتبره أيضاً مخالفاً لصريح المعقول . وينبغي أن نلاحظ أن عمل ابن تيمية لم يكن هدمياً فحسب ، بل كان فيه جانب إنشائي . فقد حاول أن يضع بجانب نقده للمنطق الأرسططاليسي منطقاً آخر إسلامياً . وهو في هذا يشبه الشكاك التجريبيين الذين كان لهم أثر عليه في بعض عناصر الجانب الهدمي من نقده للمنطق الأرسططاليسي . أما الجانب الإنشائي لنقد ابن تيمية وكثير من عناصر الجانب الهدمي فيكاد يكون
We see from all this that the influence of Ibn al-Salah was profound among Muslim scholars. But soon the critique of Aristotelian logic took another direction at the hands of the thinker of the later Salaf, Taqi al-Din Ibn Taymiyya (d. 621 AH). The attack was no longer represented in the form of fatwas decreeing its prohibition and abandonment, but the attack began to take the form of methodological criticism based on logical foundations. In other words, Ibn Taymiyya was not content with saying that logic contradicts sound transmitted knowledge (naql), but also considered it contradictory to clear rational knowledge (‘aql). It should be noted that Ibn Taymiyya's work was not only destructive, but also had a constructive side. He tried to set alongside his critique of Aristotelian logic another Islamic logic. In this he resembles the empirical skeptics who influenced him in some elements of the destructive aspect of his critique of Aristotelian logic. As for the constructive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique and many of the elements of the destructive aspect, it almost is...
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage discusses the shift in the Islamic critique of logic from Ibn al-Salah's fatwa-based prohibition to Ibn Taymiyya's methodological and rational critique. The author notes that Ibn al-Salah's influence was deep, but Ibn Taymiyya changed the direction: instead of simply issuing legal opinions forbidding logic, he engaged in a systematic criticism based on logical principles. Ibn Taymiyya argued that Aristotelian logic contradicts not only sound transmitted knowledge (sahih al-manqul, i.e., the Quran and Sunna) but also clear rational knowledge (sarih al-ma‘qul). The author then highlights the constructive dimension of Ibn Taymiyya's work: he did not merely demolish Aristotelian logic but attempted to construct an alternative Islamic logic. The author compares him to empirical skeptics who influenced his destructive criticism. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing to discuss the constructive elements. This marks a significant development in the history of Islamic logic, moving from prohibition to substantive critique and alternative system-building.

Key Terms
نقد منهجي (naqd manhaji): Methodological critique; Ibn Taymiyya's approach that analyzes logic on its own rational grounds rather than just condemning it through fatwas. [Note: This term is used here to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's approach from earlier legalistic critiques; later scholars continue this methodological analysis.]
صحيح المنقول (sahih al-manqul): Sound transmitted knowledge, i.e., the Quran and authentic Hadith; Ibn Taymiyya claims logic contradicts this. [Note: Stable concept in Islamic epistemology, but its application to logic is specific to this critique.]
صريح المعقول (sarih al-ma‘qul): Clear rational knowledge; Ibn Taymiyya argues logic also contradicts reason, not just revelation. [Note: This pairing of manqul and ma‘qul is characteristic of Ibn Taymiyya's thought.]
جانب إنشائي (janib insha'i): Constructive aspect; Ibn Taymiyya's attempt to build an alternative Islamic logic, not just destroy Aristotelian logic. [Note: This constructive project is unique to Ibn Taymiyya and later Salafi thought.]
شكاك تجريبيين (shukak tajribiyyin): Empirical skeptics; Ibn Taymiyya is compared to them regarding the destructive aspect of his critique, suggesting an influence from skeptical traditions. [Note: This term points to possible influences from Greek skepticism or empirical traditions, a topic of scholarly debate.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what grounds did Ibn Taymiyya base his critique of Aristotelian logic?
A) He issued fatwas prohibiting logic and called for its elimination.
B) He argued that logic contradicts both sound transmitted knowledge and clear rational knowledge.
C) He accepted the usefulness of logic but modified it for Islamic purposes.
D) He only used transmitted knowledge arguments against logic, ignoring reason.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya transformed the critique of logic from a legal prohibition to a rational and methodological refutation, while simultaneously attempting to construct an alternative Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: From the passage, identify two phrases that show Ibn Taymiyya's critique is methodological rather than purely legal. Write them down and explain why they indicate a shift.
?
Task 2: Consider a hadith scholar who says: 'This weak hadith contradicts both authentic transmitted reports (sahih al-manqul) and clear rational principles (sarih al-ma'qul).' In what way is this scholar adopting Ibn Taymiyya's dual approach? How might this method strengthen the critique?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Essence-Existence Distinction
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the distinction between essence and existence in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the extra-mental existence of quiddities and evaluate its implications for Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya denies that essences exist outside the mind; only individuals are real.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: the claim that quiddities (essences) have a reality outside the mind, distinct from both mental existence and particular individuals. He argues that this leads to Platonic forms and undermines tawhid. Follow his reasoning step by step to see how he dismantles this metaphysical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find the statement that quiddities (mahiyyat) have a fixed external existence separate from mental existence and particular individuals. The author says: 'that the quiddities of things... are also established in the external, then they are different from the particular existents...'
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
Ibn Taymiyya equates the logicians' claim to saying 'the non-existent is a thing' or 'it is existent,' both erroneous. He asserts that quiddities exist only in the mind.
He quotes: 'mental existence is broader than external existence...'
3
Extract his positive position
Ibn Taymiyya states: quiddity is 'what is depicted in the soul of the thing' — it exists only in minds. External existence is only of particular individuals (Zayd, 'Amr, etc.).
4
Connect to broader consequences
Affirming extra-mental quiddities leads to Platonic forms (e.g., 'humanity' and 'horseness' as eternal realities). This contradicts Islamic tawhid and supports the philosophers' argument for the eternity of the world (through eternal hyle).
Ibn Taymiyya sees this as the real corruption of logic.
5
Summarize the nominalist solution
The correct distinction: quiddity is mental representation; existence is external individuals. No universals exist in reality, only individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كثيراً من الذاتيات دون العرضيات. وهذا التكوين يستند في الحقيقة إلى تقسيم صفات المحدود إلى ذاتية وعرضية. فالذاتي المقوم هو ما كان داخلاً في الماهية، والعرضي هو ما كان خارجاً عن الذات مع كونه لازماً لها وتقسيم هذا العرضي إلى نوعين لازم للماهية ولازم لوجودها. ويعتبر المناطقة الصفات الذاتية مقدمة على الموصوف في الذهن والخارج، بل وتكون أجزاء سابقة لحقيقة الوصف نفسه في الوجودين الذهني والخارجي. ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذا الكلام يستند على أصلين فاسدين: الأصل الأول: أن ماهيات الأشياء التي هي حقائقها ثابتة أيضاً في الخارج ثم هي تغاير الموجودات المعينة الثابتة في الخارج، أي أن للماهيات وجوداً خارجياً غير وجودها الذهني ووجود أشخاصها المعينة. أي أنهم فرقوا بين الماهية ووجودها. الأصل الثاني الفرق بين الذاتي لهذه الماهية واللازم لها.

الأصل الأول: أما عن الفرق بين الماهية ووجودها فان القول بأن الماهية لها حقيقة ثابتة في الخارج غير وجودها أي أنها أمور ثابتة لا في الأذهان فقط ولكن في الخارج أيضاً يشبه القول بأن « المعدوم شيء » أو «أنه موجود» وهذان القولان خطأ عند ابن تيمية وإن ابن تيمية يرى أن الماهيات موجودة فقط في الأذهان. والوجود الذهني « أوسع من الوجود العيني، والمقدر في الأذهان موجود وثابت، وليس هو في نفس الأمر موجوداً ولا ثابتاً » فابن تيمية إذن ينكر وجود الماهيات في الخارج. وهذا النقد يتصل بالمذهب العام لابن تيمية من ناحية لأنه ينكر وجود الماهيات المجردة في الخارج من حيث أنها كلية، والعلم الوحيد لديه هو العلم الجزئي المعين ومن ناحية أخرى إن اثبات وجود الماهية في الخارج سيؤدي إلى اعتبار الحقائق النوعية كالإنسانية والفرسية « ثابتة » ثبوتاً خارجياً غير أعيانها الموجودة في الخارج، « وأزلية » لا تقبل الاستحالة والتغير وهذه هي المثل الأفلاطونية. والمثل الأفلاطونية مخالفة للتوحيد الإسلامي بالرغم من محاولات التوفيق التي قام بها بعض مفكري الإسلام. كما أنهم أيضاً حاولوا اثبات ماهيات للمادة والمكان. نتج من اثبات ماهيات للمادة مجردة عن الصور ثابتة في الخارج، أزلية الهيولي وأبديتها التي بنى عليها الفلاسفة فكرة قدم العالم. إن السبب الحقيقي لفساد المنطق هو قيامه على أساس ميتافيزيقي فما ذكروه في المنطق من الفرق بين الماهية ووجودها هو مبني على التفريق بينها في الفلسفة الأولى.

أما وجود الماهية الوحيد عند ابن تيمية فهو وجودها الذهني. وقد أنكر هذا الوجود المتكلمون، وما في الخارج عند ابن تيمية هو تحقق ما في الوجود الذهني في أعيان جزئية معينة فالموجود الوحيد في الخارج هو الشخص. أما التفريق الصحيح بين الوجود والماهية فهو أن الماهية « هي ما يرسم في النفس من الشيء » أي أنها موجودة في الأذهان لا في الأعيان. والوجود « ما يكون في الخارج منه، أي لا يوجد من هذه الماهية في الخارج إلا الأفراد والشخصيات. فليست هناك في الخارج فرسية أو انسانية، ولكن هناك زيد وعمرو وليست هناك « أعداد مجردة » كما يرى الفيثاغوريون ولا « ماهيات مجردة » كما يرى أفلاطون ولا « ماهيات مطلقة موجودة في الخارج مقارنة لوجود الأشخاص » كما يرى أرسطو ولا « معدومات ثابتة » كما يرى المتكلمون. هنا اتجاه اسمي يقصر وجود الماهيات على الذهن وينكر وجودها في الواقع. أما في الواقع فليس إلا الأفراد.
Many [attributes] from the essential ones without the accidental ones. And this composition is actually based on dividing the attributes of the limited (al-mahdud) into essential and accidental. The constitutive essential (al-dhati al-muqawwim) is what is internal to the quiddity (mahiyya), and the accidental (al-aradi) is what is external to the essence while being necessary for it, and dividing this accidental into two types: necessary for the quiddity and necessary for its existence. The logicians consider the essential attributes as prior to the described in the mind and external reality, and they are even prior parts of the reality of the description itself in both mental and external existence. Ibn Taymiyya holds that this statement is based on two corrupt foundations: The first foundation: that the quiddities of things, which are their realities, are also established in the external, then they are different from the particular existents established in the external, i.e., that quiddities have an external existence other than their mental existence and the existence of their particular individuals. That is, they distinguished between quiddity and its existence. The second foundation: the difference between the essential (dhati) of this quiddity and the necessary (lazim) for it.

The first foundation: As for the difference between quiddity and its existence... the statement that quiddity has a fixed reality in the external other than its existence — i.e., that they are fixed matters not in minds only but also in the external — resembles the statement that 'the non-existent is a thing' or 'it is existent,' and these two statements are erroneous according to Ibn Taymiyya. Indeed, Ibn Taymiyya holds that quiddities exist only in minds. And mental existence is 'broader than external existence, and what is estimated in minds is existent and fixed, but it is not in reality existent nor fixed.' So Ibn Taymiyya therefore denies the existence of quiddities in the external. This critique relates to Ibn Taymiyya's general doctrine on one hand — because he denies the existence of abstract quiddities in the external insofar as they are universal, and the only knowledge for him is determinate particular knowledge — and on the other hand, affirming the existence of quiddity in the external would lead to considering specific realities like 'humanity' and 'horseness' as 'fixed' with an external fixity other than their particular individuals existing in the external, and 'eternal' not admitting impossibility and change, and these are the Platonic forms. And the Platonic forms contradict Islamic tawhid despite the reconciliation attempts made by some Muslim thinkers. Also, they attempted to affirm quiddities for matter and place. From affirming quiddities for matter abstracted from forms, fixed in the external, resulted the eternity of hyle and its perpetuity upon which the philosophers built the idea of the world's pre-eternity. The real reason for the corruption of logic is that it is based on a metaphysical foundation; what they mentioned in logic about the difference between quiddity and its existence is built on distinguishing between them in first philosophy.

As for the only existence of quiddity according to Ibn Taymiyya, it is its mental existence. The mutakallimun denied this existence, and what is in the external according to Ibn Taymiyya is the realization of what is in mental existence in specific particular individuals — so the only existent in the external is the individual. As for the correct distinction between existence and quiddity, it is that quiddity is 'what is depicted in the soul of the thing,' i.e., it exists in minds not in external individuals. Existence is 'what is in the external of it, i.e., nothing of this quiddity exists in the external except individuals and persons. So there is no 'horseness' or 'humanity' in the external, but there are Zayd and 'Amr, and there are no 'abstract numbers' as the Pythagoreans hold, nor 'abstract quiddities' as Plato holds, nor 'absolute quiddities existing in the external concomitant with the existence of individuals' as Aristotle holds, nor 'fixed non-existents' as the mutakallimun hold. Here is a nominalist direction that restricts the existence of quiddities to the mind and denies their existence in reality. As for reality, there are only individuals.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critical analysis of two foundational principles underlying Aristotelian logic, focusing on the first principle: the distinction between essence (mahiyya) and existence (wujud). The author begins by outlining the logicians' standard view that attributes are divided into essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi), where the essential constitutes the quiddity and the accidental is external yet necessary. The logicians prioritize essential attributes in both mental and external existence. Ibn Taymiyya then identifies two corrupt foundations behind this scheme. The first foundation is the claim that quiddities (essences) have a fixed reality externally, distinct from both mental existence and particular individuals. Ibn Taymiyya compares this to the erroneous doctrines that 'the non-existent is a thing' or that it is existent. He argues that quiddities exist only in the mind, citing that mental existence is broader than external existence. He further links this critique to his rejection of Platonic forms, which he sees as a consequence of affirming extra-mental quiddities, and which contradict Islamic tawhid. He also connects this to the philosophers' argument for the eternity of the world based on the eternity of matter. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the real corruption of logic stems from its metaphysical foundation. He then clarifies his own position: quiddity exists only in the mind, and external existence consists only of particular individuals. The correct distinction is that quiddity is a mental representation, while existence is the external realization of individuals. The passage ends by noting that this position is nominalist, restricting quiddities to the mind.

Key Terms
الماهية
الوجود
الذاتي
العرضي

B) Mental existence
C) Both mental and external existence
D) Neither mental nor external existence

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reduces quiddity to a mental concept, denying any extra-mental reality to universals, thereby severing the link between logic and metaphysics.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Aristotelian Logic on Mutawatirat
Author critiques Aristotelian logic's denial of mutawatirat and defends their certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic's treatment of mutawatirat and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
Denying mutawatirat confuses ignorance of a thing with proof of its non-existence.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Aristotelian logicians' claim that mutawatirat are only certain for those who personally receive them undermines the entire structure of transmitted religious knowledge. He exposes a crucial logical fallacy: treating absence of evidence as evidence of absence. In this lesson, you will dissect his argument and see how it connects to the broader Islamic critique of Greek logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find what the Aristotelian logicians assert about mutawatirat. They claim that mutawatirat are exclusive to those who directly know them, and not universally binding.
Look for phrases like 'يختص بها من علمها' (exclusive to those who know it).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's logical distinction
Identify the phrase 'absence of knowledge, not knowledge of absence' (عدم العلم لا العلم بالعدم). What does this distinction mean in logical terms?
It refutes the fallacy of arguing from ignorance.
3
Apply the distinction to the denial of mutawatirat
Explain how the denier's lack of evidence for a mutawatir report does not prove the report is false. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the opponent only has 'absence of knowledge'.
Contrast this with the logicians' claim that mutawatirat are not proven.
4
Trace the consequences for religious knowledge
What does Ibn Taymiyya say is at stake? He links mutawatirat to prophethood, miracles, and the entire transmitted tradition.
The denial undermines the foundations of Islam.
5
Identify the Islamic institutional response
How did Muslims respond to this challenge? The author mentions the development of mustalah al-hadith as a decisive response.
This science provides rigorous criteria for verifying reports.
